Multiple Causes of Death: Proportional Liability in Hanafi Diyat
Ruling on liability when multiple agents contribute to a death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Zayd is liable for one-third diya when multiple causes contribute to a death, and apply the Hanafi principle of categorizing causes by their worldly and religious effect.

Core Idea
Causes of death are categorized by worldly and Hereafter effect; only fully considered causes bear diya proportionally.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents a complex causation scenario: a man dies from his own action, the action of Zayd, a lion, and a snake. The ruling that Zayd owes one-third diya is not arbitrary; it is derived from a detailed classification of each cause by its legal and religious consequences. Understanding this reasoning is key to apportioning liability in multi-cause homicide cases under Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the passage: a person dies due to his own action, Zayd's action, a lion, and a snake. The question is: what is Zayd's liability for diya?
2
Categorize each cause by hadar status
The lion and snake are hadar (negligible) in both this world and the Hereafter. The victim's own action is hadar in this world (no worldly penalty) but sinful in the Hereafter. Zayd's action is fully considered in both worlds.
Hadar means no worldly legal consequence.
3
Group causes into types
Lion+snake form one type (both hadar), the self forms a second type (partially considered), Zayd forms a third (fully considered). So three categories.
Multiple causes with same hadar status are grouped.
4
Attribute the death equally among types
Since all three types contributed to the death, each is responsible for one-third of the causation.
Even hadar causes are counted for division.
5
Determine liability from the type with worldly consequence
Only Zayd's category carries worldly liability, so he must pay one-third of the diya. The victim's own action has no worldly liability, and the animals have none.
If Zayd's action was intentional, diya from his wealth; if accidental, from his 'aqila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن مات بفعل نفسه وزيد وأسد وحية، ضمن زيد ثلث الدية. ضمن زيد إلخ: لأن فعل الأسد والحية جنس واحد؛ لكونه هدرا في الدنيا والآخرة، وفعله بنفسه جنس آخر؛ لكونه هدرا في الدنيا معتبرا في الآخرة، حتى يأثم به، وفعل زيد معتبر في الدنيا والآخرة، فصار ثلاثة أجناس: ومعتبر ومعتبر من وجه دون وجه وهو فعله بنفسه، فيكون الثابت فعلا واحدا، فيجب على زيد ثلث الدية، ثم إن كان فعل زيد عمدا تجب عليه الدية في ماله وإلا فعلى العاقلة تكملة البحر الرائق: ٧٨/٩]
And whoever dies by his own action and Zayd's and a lion and a snake, then Zayd is liable for one-third of the diya. 'Zayd is liable etc.': because the action of the lion and the snake are of one type; since they are negligible (hadar) in this world and the Hereafter. And his own action is another type; since it is negligible in this world but considered in the Hereafter, such that he sins by it. And Zayd's action is considered in this world and the Hereafter. Thus there are three types: considered, and considered from one perspective but not another (which is his own action). So what is established is one act (of killing), so Zayd owes one-third of the diya. Then if Zayd's action was intentional, the diya is due from his own wealth; otherwise it is upon the 'aqila. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/78]
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage addresses a complex causation scenario where multiple agents contribute to a person's death: the victim himself (by some self-inflicted action), a man named Zayd, a lion, and a snake. The question is the liability of Zayd. The ruling is that Zayd is only liable for one-third of the diya (blood money). The reasoning, explained in the commentary, involves categorizing the actions based on their status in worldly and religious law. The lion and snake are considered 'hadar' (negligible) both in this world and the Hereafter—there is no worldly punishment or religious sin for their actions (since animals are not accountable). The victim's own action is also negligible in this world (he cannot be punished for his own death) but it is considered in the Hereafter as a sin if he committed suicide. Zayd's action is fully considered both in this world (liable for diya or qisas if intentional) and in the Hereafter (sinful if intentional or negligent). Thus, we have three distinct categories of causation: (1) fully negligible (lion/snake), (2) partially considered (self), (3) fully considered (Zayd). When these combine to cause a single death, the death is attributed equally to each cause? Actually, the text says 'what is established is one act' — meaning the death is one event, and liability is divided among the effective causes. Since the lion/snake contribute no liability, Zayd and the victim's own act are the only two effective causes. But Zayd's act is fully considered while the victim's act is only partially considered (has worldly immunity). The text concludes Zayd pays one-third, implying a division into three equal parts? Or perhaps the reasoning is that the lion and snake together count as one cause (since both are hadar), the self as another, and Zayd as the third, so three causes, each contributing equally to the death, but only Zayd's cause carries worldly liability, so he pays one-third. The commentary also distinguishes that if Zayd's act was intentional, the diya is from his own wealth; if it was accidental, the diya is upon his 'aqila (tribal guarantors).

Key Terms
هدر (hadar): Negligible; actions that carry no worldly legal consequences (punishment or compensation) and no religious sin (like animal actions).
العاقلة (al-'aqila): The 'akila are the male agnate relatives (tribe or kin) who bear the diyah for accidental or quasi-intentional homicide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling from this passage, why does Zayd pay one-third of the diya rather than half or full?
A) Because the lion and snake are considered separate causes, making three total causes.
B) Because Zayd's action is considered fully in this world and the Hereafter, while the victim's and animals' actions are hadar in at least one world.
C) Because the lion and snake are exempt, leaving only Zayd and the victim, but the victim's action reduces Zayd's liability by half.
D) Because Zayd committed a minor act.

Key Takeaway
In multi-cause homicides under Hanafi fiqh, causes are grouped by their legal/religious status, and liability is divided equally among these groups, with only the fully considered agents paying diya proportionally.

When Pardoning the Cut Does Not Pardon the Death: Diyah by Istihsan
Ruling on diyah when victim pardons the cutting then dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the effects of a victim's pardon of a cutting and pardon of the cutting and its consequence, and apply the Hanafi rule on diyah when the victim later dies.

Core Idea
When a victim pardons only the cut, diyah remains due; if the pardon explicitly covers the consequence, no diyah is owed — the wording determines the ruling.

In Hanafi fiqh, a victim's pardon of a physical injury can raise complex questions when that injury leads to death. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies whether a pardon of the cutting (e.g., amputation) covers the subsequent death from the same wound. The ruling depends on the exact wording of the pardon and the application of istihsan (juristic preference) over strict qiyas. We will also see how the victim's health status at the time of pardon affects financial liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
A victim is cut (e.g., hand amputated) and later dies from that wound. Before death, the victim pardons the cutter. What is the effect on qisas (retaliation) or diyah (blood money)?
The key variable is the wording of the pardon: does it mention only the cut, or does it include the consequence?
2
Understand the qiyas vs istihsan
Strict qiyas would suggest that a pardon of the cut is irrelevant to the death, so qisas should apply for the killing. However, istihsan prefers diyah due to the unity of the act (cutting and death are one continuous event), raising a suspicion that the pardon might have encompassed the death.
This istihsan is the basis for Abu Hanifa's ruling that diyah is due, not qisas.
3
Compare Abu Hanifa with the Sahibayn
According to Abu Hanifa, if the victim says only 'I pardon the cut,' the cutter still owes diyah for the death because the pardon did not explicitly cover the fatal consequence. The Sahibayn hold that the phrase 'I pardon the cut' implies forgiving its consequence (since the cut's natural result is either healing or death), so no diyah is due.
If the victim explicitly says 'I pardon the cut and what occurs from it' or 'I pardon the offense,' both agree no diyah is due.
4
Apply the sick-person limitation
When the victim is terminally ill at the time of pardon, the pardon is treated as a donation (tabarru'). For accidental injuries, the diyah is wealth belonging to the heirs, so the pardon is effective only from one-third of the victim's estate. For intentional injuries, the right is qisas (not wealth), so the pardon can exhaust the entire estate.
This ensures that heirs' rights are not entirely overridden by a deathbed donation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضمن القاطع إلخ لأنه عفا عن القطع، وهو غير القتل، وبالسراية تبين أن حقه القتل لا القطع، فيضمن القاطع الدية استحسانا وفي القياس ينبغي أن يجب القصاص؛ لأن العفو والحالة هذه عفو عن غير حقه، لكن في الاستحسان تحب الدية؛ لأن العفو وإن صادف القطع دون القتل إلا أن الفعل متحد صورة، فتمكن شبهة العفو، وعندهما: لا يضمن الدية؛ لأنه يراد به العفو عن موجبه لا ،ذاته وموجبه قطع إن اقتصر، وقتل إن سرى، فيتناول أيهما وجد، مر وجه قول الإمام. (فتح) لا : أي لا يضمن الدية في هذه الصورة؛ لأن اسم الجناية يتناول القطع والقتل جميعا، وقوله: "ما يحدث عنه" أي من القطع تصريح بالعفو عن السراية، فكان عفوا عن القتل. (فتح) من الثلث إلخ يعني إذا عفا المقطوع القاطع بأن قال: عفوت عن القطع وما يحدث منها أو عفوت عن الجناية، ثم مات المقطوع بهذا القطع صح عفوه عن القتل، ولا يضمن القاطع الدية إلا أنه لما كان هذا العفو تبرعا، وتبرع المريض ينفذ في الثلث، فإذا كان قطع القاطع خطأ يعتبر عفوه من ثلث ماله؛ لأن موجب الخطأ المال وقد تعلق به حق الورثة، فيعتبر بالثلث كسائر أمواله، وإذا كان عمدا يعتبر من جميع المال؛ لأن موجبه القصاص، وذلك ليس بمال، فلم يتعلق به حق الورثة، ولما كان العفو في الخطأ معتبرا من الثلث، فإن خرج من الثلث فيها، وإلا فعلى عاقلة القاطع ثلثا الدية إن لم يكن للعافي مال غيرها، وإن كان فبحسابه. (ملتقطا من فالخطأ
'He is liable for the diyah' etc., because he pardoned the cut, which is not the killing. By the fatality, it becomes clear that his right was the killing, not the cut, so the cutter is liable for diyah by istihsan. By qiyas, it would be that qisas is due, because pardon in this case is a pardon of what is not his right, but by istihsan, diyah is preferred because the pardon, though it fell on the cut rather than the killing, the act is a single entity, so a suspicion of pardon arises. According to the two (Sahibayn), he is not liable for diyah because he intended the pardon of its consequence, not the act itself, and its consequence is cut if limited, and killing if it spreads, so it applies to whichever occurs. (From Fath) 'No': i.e., he is not liable for diyah in this case because the name 'offense' includes both the cut and the killing, and his saying 'what occurs from it' i.e., from the cut, is an explicit pardon for the fatality, so it is a pardon of the killing. (From Fath) 'From the third' etc. means that if the victim of the cut pardons the cutter by saying 'I pardon the cut and what occurs from it' or 'I pardon the offense', then the victim dies from that cut, his pardon of the killing is valid, and the cutter is not liable for diyah. However, since this pardon is a donation, and the donation of a sick person is effective only within one-third of his wealth, so if the cut was accidental, his pardon is considered from one-third of his wealth because the consequence of the accidental act is wealth, and the heirs have a right to it. If it is intentional, it is considered from all his wealth because its consequence is qisas, which is not wealth, so the heirs have no right. Since the pardon in the accidental case is from one-third, if it is within one-third, it is valid; otherwise, the cutter's 'aqilah pays two-thirds of the diyah if the pardoner has no other wealth, otherwise according to calculation. (Compiled from al-Fath, so the accidental is from one-third, and the intentional from all wealth.)
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of a victim pardoning the cutting (amputation) when he later dies from that cut. The key issue is whether the pardon of the cut also pardons the resulting death. According to istihsan, if the victim pardons only the cut (not the possible death), then when he dies, the cutter must pay diyah, because the right proved to be the death, which was not pardoned. The qiyas would require qisas, but istihsan sets diyah due to the suspicion that the pardon encompassed the death since the act is one. The Sahibayn disagree: they say that if the victim says 'I pardon the cut and what occurs from it' or 'I pardon the offense', that includes the fatal consequence, so no diyah is due. Another sub-issue: the pardon by a sick person is a donation, effective only in one-third of his wealth. For an accidental cut, the diyah is wealth, so the pardon is limited to one-third; the remaining two-thirds are due from the cutter's 'aqilah. For an intentional cut, the consequence is qisas (not wealth), so the pardon can be from all his wealth since heirs have no right in qisas. The passage thus covers both the substantive ruling and the financial implications.

Key Terms
عفو (afw): Pardon or forgiveness by the victim that waives the right to qisas or financial compensation.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from strict qiyas due to a stronger consideration, here the unity of the act leading to suspicion.
سراية (sirayah): The spread of the injury leading to death; the fatality of the wound.
عاقلة ('aqilah): The group of male relatives (tribe or helpers) who are liable to pay diyah for accidental killings.
تبرع (tabarru'): A voluntary donation; a pardon is considered a donation for the purposes of the law regarding the sick person's estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a terminally ill victim of an accidental amputation pardons the cut (without mentioning the consequence) and then dies from the wound, what is the limitation on the pardon's effectiveness?
A) The pardon is effective from the entire wealth of the victim.
B) The pardon is effective only up to one-third of the victim's wealth.
C) The pardon is not effective at all because the heirs have rights.
D) The pardon is effective only if the victim explicitly mentions the consequence.

Key Takeaway
The effect of a pardon on diyah depends on the wording and whether it covers the consequence; istihsan may replace qiyas to ensure balanced justice, and a sick person's pardon is limited to one-third of the estate for accidental injuries.

The Principle of Considering the Time of Shooting for Homicide Liability
Establishes principle and applies to intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that liability is based on the act of shooting, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding apostasy after shooting.

Core Idea
Liability attaches at the moment of the act, not the moment of death; the shooter's act creates obligation regardless of the victim's later state.

In Hanafi criminal law, the timing of the act is decisive for establishing liability. This passage introduces the key principle that the state of the shooter and target at the time of shooting (ramy) determines diya and qima, not their state at death. This principle is then applied to several cases, including apostasy, emancipation, and witness retraction, revealing important disagreements within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Read the opening statement: 'المعتبر حالة الرمي' – the state at the time of shooting is what matters. Understand that this is because the shooting is the act within the agent's power (فعل الرامي).
This principle applies to both liability (damān) and permissibility (ḥill).
2
Apply to the Apostasy Case
Consider the scenario: a Muslim shoots a Muslim, then the target apostatizes before the arrow hits. Identify Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling (diya wajib) and the Sahibayn's ruling (nothing).
The passage presents the dispute clearly; note the different reasoning.
3
Analyze the Disagreement
Compare the proofs: Imam argues the act (ramy) creates liability, so later loss of sanctity is irrelevant. The Sahibayn argue that at death the target has no sanctity, so the shooter is discharged. Assess the strength of each position.
The passage also provides an analogy to hunting to support Imam's view.
4
Extend to the Slave Emancipation Case
Now apply the principle to a slave who is shot then freed before the arrow hits. Note three views: Imam (full value), Muhammad (only difference in value), Zufar (diya because free at death).
This shows the principle is applied consistently: Imam considers the act, others consider the time of hitting.
5
Apply to Stoning Witness Retraction
Understand the case: a condemned man is stoned, then a witness retracts before the stone hits. The shooter is not liable because the victim's blood was lawful at the time of throwing. However, the retracting witnesses pay diya.
This confirms the principle: the act (throwing) determines liability for the thrower.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتبر حالة الرمي، فتجب الدية بردة المرمي إليه قبل الوصول، لا بإسلامه، والقيمة بعتقه، ولا يضمن الرامي برجوع شاهد الرجم بعد الرمي. باب في اعتبار إلخ لما كانت الأحوال صفات لذواتها ذكرها بعد القتل وما يتعلق به. [تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٢٤/٩] المعتبر حالة الرمي: لأنه فعل الرامي، فوجب اعتباره في حق الحل والضمان. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٧/٢] ونوقض بما إذا رمى إلى صيد في الحل فدخل الحرم ثم أصابه السهم فمات وجب الجزاء على الرامي، وأجيب بأن جزاء الصيد لا يختص بالفعل، ولهذا تجب بدلالة المحرم وإشارته وهذا لا يكون أقل من ذلك. (فتح) فتجب الدية إلخ: [تفريع على ما ذكر من أن المعتبر حالة الرمي] يعني لو رمى رجل رجلا مسلما، فارتد المرمي إليه – والعياذ بالله – قبل وصول السهم إليه، ثم وقع به السهم تجب على الرامي الدية، وهذا عند الإمام، وقالا: لا شيء عليه؛ لأن التلف حصل في محل لا عصمة له؛ لأنه بارتداده أسقط تقوم نفسه، فصار مبرئا للرامي عن موجبه كما لو أبرأه في هذه الحالة وللإمام أن الضمان يجب بفعله وهو الرمي؛ لأنه هو الذي يدخل تحت قدرته دون الإصابة، ولا فعل له أصلا، بعده فيصير قاتلا، بالرمي، ألا ترى أنه لو رمى إلى صيد، وهو مسلم، ثم ارتد - والعياذ بالله تعالى - فأصاب السهم الصيد، وهو مرتد، فجرحه ومات بالجرح، حل أكله وكان القياس أن يجب القصاص لما ذكرنا لكنه سقط للشبهة. [تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٢٤/٩] لا بإسلامه أي لا يجب شيء بإسلام المرمي إليه بأن رمى إلى حربي أو مرتد فأسلم قبل الإصابة، ثم أصابه بعد ما أسلم، وهذا بالإجماع؛ لأن الرمي لم ينعقد موجبا للضمان لعدم تقوم المحل؛ لأن المرتد والحربي لا عصمة لدمهما. [تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٢٤/٩] والقيمة بعتقه يعني لو رمى إلى عبد، فأعتقه المولى بعد الرمي قبل الإصابة، فأصابه، فمات، لزم الرامي القيمة عند الإمام، وقال محمد: له فضل ما بين قيمته مرميا وغير مرمي؛ لأن العتق قاطع للسراية، وإذا انقطعت بقي مجرد الرمي، وهي جناية تنتقص بها قيمة المرمي إليه بالإضافة إلى ما قبل الرمي، فيجب عليه ذلك. [تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٢٤/٩] وله: أنه قاتلا له يصير من وقت الرمي، وهو مملوك في تلك الحالة، فتجب القيمة، وقال زفر عليه الدية؛ لأن الرمي إنما صار علة عند الإصابة، وهو حر في ذلك الوقت، فتجب ديته. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٧/٢] ولا يضمن الرامي إلخ الذي رمى المقضى عليه بالرجم] معناه إذا قضى القاضي برجم رجل، فرماه رجل، ثم رجع أحد الشهود بعد الرمي قبل الإصابة، ووقع عليه الحجر، فلا شيء على الرامي؛ لما أن المعتبر حالة الرمي وهو مباح الدم. [تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٢٤/٩] لكن يجب الضمان على الراجع إن رجعوا جميعا فعليهم الدية، وإن رجع واحد، فعليه ربعها. (فتح)
The state at the time of shooting is considered. So the blood money (diya) is obligatory by the apostasy of the target before the arrow reaches [him], not by his [later] acceptance of Islam. And the value (qima) [is obligatory] by his emancipation. And the shooter is not liable by the retraction of the witness of stoning after the shooting before the [stone] hits. The chapter 'On considering the state of killing' etc. Since conditions are attributes of their subjects, he mentioned them after killing and what pertains to it. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/124] 'The state at the time of shooting is considered': because it is the act of the shooter, so its consideration is obligatory regarding permissibility and liability. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/437] It was objected with [the case] when someone shot at game in the halal (permissible area), then [the game] entered the sanctuary, then the arrow hit it and it died: the penalty (jaza') is obligatory on the shooter. And it was answered that the penalty for game is not specific to the act, for this reason it is obligatory by the pointing and signaling of the muhrim, and this is not less than that. (Fath) 'So the blood money is obligatory' etc.: [a branch on what was mentioned that the state at the time of shooting is considered] means that if a man shot a Muslim, and the target apostatized – God forbid – before the arrow reached him, then the arrow struck him, the blood money is obligatory on the shooter according to Imam [Abu Hanifa]. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: nothing is obligatory on him, because the destruction occurred in a locus with no inviolability; since by his apostasy he abolished the value of his person, so he became a discharger of the shooter from his obligation, just as if he had discharged him in this state. And for Imam: liability is obligatory by his act, which is the shooting, because it is what falls under his ability, not the hitting, and he has no act after it at all. So he becomes a killer by the shooting. Do you not see that if he shot at game while he was Muslim, then apostatized – God forbid – and the arrow hit the game while it was [still] an apostate, wounded it and it died from the wound, it became permissible to eat it, and the analogy would be that retaliation (qisas) would be obligatory for what we mentioned, but it is dropped due to the doubt. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/124] 'Not by his Islam': meaning, nothing is obligatory by the Islam of the target, such as when he shot at a harbi (enemy combatant) or apostate, then he became Muslim before the hit, then [the arrow] hit him after he had become Muslim. And this is by consensus, because the shooting did not become a cause for liability due to the lack of value of the locus, because the apostate and harbi have no sanctity for their blood. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/124] 'And the value by his emancipation': meaning, if he shot at a slave, and the master emancipated him after the shooting before the hit, then the arrow hit him and he died, the shooter is bound to pay the value according to Imam. And Muhammad said: he pays only the difference between his value before being shot and after being shot; because emancipation is a blocker of the transmission (of liability), and when it is blocked, only the mere shooting remains, and it is an injury that decreases the value of the target in relation to before the shooting, so he must pay that. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/124] And for him (Imam): he becomes a killer from the time of the shooting, and he was a slave at that time, so the value is obligatory. Zufar said: blood money is obligatory on him; because the shooting becomes a cause only at the time of hitting, and he was free at that time, so his blood money is obligatory. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/437] 'And the shooter is not liable' etc. [meaning the one who threw the stone at the person sentenced to stoning]: its meaning is that if the judge gave a ruling to stone a man, then a man stoned him, then one of the witnesses retracted after the stoning before the stone hit, and the stone struck him, then nothing is on the shooter; because the state at the time of shooting is considered, and his blood was permissible. [Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/124] But liability is obligatory on the retracting witness: if they all retract, the blood money is upon them; if one retracts, one-fourth of it is upon him. (Fath)
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
The passage begins by stating the principle: the state at the time of shooting (ramy) is what determines liability in killing, not the state at the time of death. This is because the shooting is the act within the agent's power. The author then branches into several applications. First, if the victim was Muslim at the time of shooting but apostatized before the arrow hit, according to Imam Abu Hanifa the diya (blood money) is obligatory, because the act of shooting created liability. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, arguing that since the victim lost sanctity before death, the shooter is discharged, like an explicit discharge. The author supports Imam's view by analogy to hunting: if a Muslim shoots game then apostatizes, the game is still halal, indicating the act counts. Second, if the target was a non-Muslim (harbi or apostate) and becomes Muslim before the arrow hits, there is consensus that no liability arises, because the shooting never created any liability due to lack of sanctity. Third, if the target was a slave and is freed after shooting but before the arrow hits, Imam says the full value (qima) is due, while Muhammad says only the decrease in value caused by the shooting is due, and Zufar says diya because he was free at death. The passage then discusses retraction of stoning witnesses: if one stoner throws a stone at a condemned person, and then witnesses retract before the stone hits, the stoner is not liable (since the victim was legally blood-available), but the retracting witnesses must pay diya proportionally.

Key Terms
الرمي (al-ramy): The act of shooting or throwing an arrow, stone, or projectile; the physical act that initiates the chain of causation.
الدية (al-diya): Blood money; financial compensation paid by the killer or his 'aajila to the victim's heirs for homicide or bodily injury.
المرمى إليه (al-marmā ilayh): The target; the person or object at which the shooting is directed.
القيمة (al-qima): Market value of a slave; here, the compensation due when a slave is killed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa regarding a shooter who shoots a Muslim, who then apostatizes before the arrow hits?
A) The shooter owes nothing because the target lost sanctity.
B) The shooter owes diya because the act of shooting created liability.
C) The shooter owes qisas (retaliation).
D) The shooter owes only one-fourth of diya.

Key Takeaway
The state at the time of the act (ramy) determines liability in killing, not the state at death; this principle is applied consistently across cases, though there are intra-Hanafi disputes on its implications.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
10 min
Blood Money for Quasi-Intentional and Accidental Killing
Defines diyat and details camel compositions for shibh al-amd and khata'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for diyah in shibh al-amd and khata', identify the intra-Hanafi dispute over the composition for shibh al-amd, and explain the proofs from hadith.

Core Idea
Blood money for shibh al-amd is 100 camels in four quarters, and for khata' 100 in five fifths—but the composition of the quarters is disputed among Hanafi imams.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq introduces the Book of Blood Money. It defines diyah and then details the amounts and camel compositions for two categories of homicide: quasi-intentional (shibh al-amd) and accidental (khata'). The text also reveals an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani over the exact composition for shibh al-amd. Understanding these rulings and their proofs is essential for anyone dealing with Islamic criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of homicide
The passage discusses shibh al-amd (quasi-intentional) and khata' (accidental). Note the definitions in the commentary: shibh al-amd is killing with an intentional act but no intent to kill (e.g., whip, stick), while khata' is purely accidental.
2
State the ruling for shibh al-amd
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two): 100 camels in four equal categories of 25: bint makhad, bint labun, hiqqa, jadha'a. Imam Muhammad differs, requiring 30 hiqqa, 30 jadha'a, and 40 pregnant thinniya.
The mufta bihi is the view of the two.
3
State the ruling for khata'
All agree: 100 camels in five categories of 20: ibn makhad, bint makhad, bint labun, hiqqa, jadha'a. This is established by a hadith from Ibn Mas'ud.
The hadith explicitly enumerates these categories.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad cites a hadith: '40 from thinniya to bazil, all pregnant.' The two rely on a hadith that the Prophet judged 100 camels in quarters. The text explains that since the quarter division does not fit khata' (which is fifths), the quarters hadith must refer to shibh al-amd.
The two's hadith is considered more explicit and is preferred.
5
Evaluate the proofs and note taghliz
The text notes that taghliz (intensification) applies only to camels; no other commodity can substitute. This is a tawqifi (revelation-based) estimation. If a judge rules otherwise, his judgment is void.

كتاب الديات أحكام الديات دية شبه العمد مائة من الإبل أرباعا من بنت مخاض إلى جذعة، ولا تغليظ إلا في الإبل، والخطأ مائة من الإبل أخماسا : ابن مخاض وبنت مخاض وبنت لبون وحقة وجذعة، دية الخطأ عشرون عشرون عشرون عشرون عشرون كتاب الديات: الديات جمع دية، وهي في اللغة مصدر، ودى القاتل المقتول أعطى ديته وأعطى لوليه المال الذي هو بدل النفس، ثم قيل لذلك المال : الدية تسمية بالمصدر كذا في "المغرب"، وفي الشرع: عبارة عما يؤدي، وقد صار هذا الاسم علما على بدل النفوس دون غيرها، وهو الأرش، وإنما سمي بدل النفس دية؛ لأنه يؤدي عادة؛ لأنه قلما يجري فيه العفو؛ لعظم حرمة الآدمي، ثم ذكر الديات بعد الجنايات ظاهر المناسبة لما أن الدية أحد موجي الجناية في الآدمي صيانة له عن القصاص، لكن لما كان معنى الإحياء في القصاص أكثر، قدم بيان القود على الدية. تكملة، فتح، ملا مسكين) مائة من الإبل، الإبل إلخ: أي دية شبه العمد مائة من الإبل أرباعا من بنت مخاض، وهي التي طعنت في السنة الثانية إلى جذعة، وهي التي طعنت في الخامسة، وتكون خمس وعشرون بنت مخاض، وخمس وعشرون بنت لبون، وخمس وعشرون حقة، وخمس وعشرون جذعة، وهذا عندهما وعند محمد ثلاثون حقة، وثلاثون جذعة، وأربعون ثنية في بطونها أولادها؛ لقوله عليه: "ألا إن قتيل خطأ العمد بالسوط والعصا ،والحجر، وفيه دية مغلظة مائة ، من أربعون منها من ثنية إلى بازل عامها كلهن خلفة، وبه قال الشافعي وأحمد في رواية، ولهما: أن النبي عليه قضى في الدية بمائة من الإبل ،أرباعا، ومعلوم أنه لم يرد به الخطأ؛ لأنها تجب فيه أحماسا، فعلم أن المراد به شبه العمد والثني من الإبل ما استكمل السنة الخامسة ودخل في السادسة، والبازل ما دخل في السنة التاسعة، والذكر والأنثى فيه سواء، والخلفة الحامل من النوق. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٨/٢] ولا تغليظ إلخ لأن الشرع ورد ،به وعليه الإجماع والمقدرات لا تعرف إلا سماعا؛ إذ لا مدخل للرأي فيها، فلم تتغلظ بغيره حتى لو قضى به القاضي لا ينفذ قضاؤه؛ لعدم التوقيف بالتقدير بغير الإبل تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٢٧/٩] والخطأ إلخ: أي دية الخطأ مائة من الإبل أحماسا أي عشرون ابن مخاض وعشرون بنت مخاض وعشرون بنت لبون وعشرون حقة، وعشرون جذعة تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۲۸/۹] لما روى ابن مسعود همه أن النبي قال: "في دية الخطأ عشرون حقة وعشرون جذعة وعشرون بنت مخاض وعشرون بنت لبون وعشرون ابن مخاض ذكر"، رواه أبو داود والترمذي ،وآخرون والشافعي أخذ بمذهبنا غير أنه قال: يجب عشرون ابن لبون مكان ابن مخاض والحديث حجة عليه رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٨/٢]
Book of Blood Money. Rulings of Blood Money. The blood money for quasi-intentional killing (shibh al-amd) is one hundred camels in quarters, from bint makhad to jadha'a, and no intensification except in camels. And accidental killing (khata') is one hundred camels in fifths: ibn makhad, bint makhad, bint labun, hiqqa, and jadha'a. (Diagram: twenty twenty twenty twenty twenty). Book of Blood Money: al-diyat is the plural of diyah, which linguistically is a verbal noun. 'He paid blood money to the killer of the slain, giving his blood money and giving to his heir the wealth which is the substitute for the life.' Then that wealth was called al-diyah as a name derived from the verbal noun. This is in al-Mughrib. Legally, it is an expression for what is paid, and this name has become a proper noun for the substitute for lives, not others, and it is al-arsh. It was named substitute for life as blood money because it is usually paid; because forgiveness rarely occurs due to the great sanctity of the human being. Then mentioning blood money after offenses is clearly appropriate because blood money is one of the consequences of the offense against a human being to protect him from retaliation. But since the meaning of giving life in retaliation is more, the explanation of retaliation was placed before blood money. (Takmila, Fath, Mulla Miskin). 'One hundred camels, camels, etc.' means: The blood money for quasi-intentional killing is one hundred camels in quarters from bint makhad, which is one that has entered its second year, to jadha'a, which is one that has entered its fifth year, consisting of twenty-five bint makhad, twenty-five bint labun, twenty-five hiqqa, and twenty-five jadha'a. This is according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). According to Muhammad, it is thirty hiqqa, thirty jadha'a, and forty thinniya (camel in its sixth year) which are pregnant with their young, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Indeed the slain of quasi-intentional by whip, stick, and stone has a severe blood money of one hundred camels, of which forty are from thinniya to bazil, all of them pregnant.' Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad (in one narration) held this view. The two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) have that the Prophet (peace be upon him) judged regarding blood money as one hundred camels in quarters, and it is known that he did not intend accidental killing because it is required in fifths; so it is understood that he meant quasi-intentional. Al-thanni from camels is one that has completed the fifth year and entered the sixth. Al-bazil is one that has entered the ninth year. The male and female are the same in it. Al-khalifah is the pregnant she-camel. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/438). 'No intensification except' because the law has come with it and there is consensus on it, and legal estimations are only known by hearing, as there is no room for opinion in them, so it is not intensified with other than it; even if a judge were to judge with it, his judgment would not be executed due to lack of authoritative prescription for estimation with other than camels. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 9/127). 'And accidental, etc.' means: The blood money for accidental killing is one hundred camels in fifths, i.e., twenty ibn makhad, twenty bint makhad, twenty bint labun, twenty hiqqa, and twenty jadha'a. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 9/128) Because of what Ibn Mas'ud narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'In accidental blood money: twenty hiqqa, twenty jadha'a, twenty bint makhad, twenty bint labun, and twenty male ibn makhad.' Reported by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, and others. Al-Shafi'i adopted our school except that he said: twenty ibn labun are required instead of ibn makhad. The hadith is evidence against him. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/438)
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage from the commentary introduces the Book of Blood Money (Kitab al-Diyat). It defines diyah linguistically as a verbal noun meaning the payment given to the heirs of the victim, and legally as the compensation for the loss of life. The text then details the amount of blood money for two types of homicide recognized in Hanafi law: quasi-intentional (shibh al-amd) and accidental (khata'). For shibh al-amd, the blood money is 100 camels divided into four categories of 25 each: bint makhad (second-year female), bint labun (third-year female), hiqqa (fourth-year female), and jadha'a (fifth-year female). This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two). Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, holding that it should be 30 hiqqa, 30 jadha'a, and 40 pregnant thinniya (sixth-year). He cites a hadith: 'The slain of quasi-intentional by whip, stick, and stone has a severe blood money of 100 camels, of which forty are from thinniya to bazil, all pregnant.' Imam al-Shafi'i and Ahmad (in one narration) followed this view. The two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) rely on a different hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) judged blood money as 100 camels in quarters. The text explains that since accidental killing requires fifths, the hadith about quarters must refer to shibh al-amd. The text also notes that the intensification (taghliz) of blood money applies only to camels; it cannot be commuted to other property even if a judge rules otherwise. For accidental killing, the blood money is also 100 camels but divided into five categories of 20 each: ibn makhad (second-year male), bint makhad, bint labun, hiqqa, and jadha'a. This is established by a hadith from Ibn Mas'ud. Imam al-Shafi'i agreed with the categories except he substituted ibn labun for ibn makhad, but the hadith supports the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money; the wealth paid as compensation for the loss of a life.
شبه العمد (shibh al-amd): Quasi-intentional killing; killing with an act intended to strike but without intention to kill (e.g., striking with a whip).
خطأ (khata'): Accidental killing; death caused unintentionally.
بنت مخاض (bint makhad): A female camel that has entered its second year.
ابن مخاض (ibn makhad): A male camel that has entered its second year.
بنت لبون (bint labun): A female camel that has entered its third year.
حقة (hiqqa): A female camel that has entered its fourth year.
جذعة (jadha'a): A female camel that has entered its fifth year.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many camels are due as blood money for accidental killing (khata')?
A) 100 camels in four categories of 25.

D) 100 camels in three categories.

Key Takeaway
Blood money for quasi-intentional killing (shibh al-amd) is 100 camels in four quarters (25 each) according to the relied-upon Hanafi view, while accidental killing (khata') requires 100 camels in five fifths (20 each), with a clear hadith as proof.

Diyat — Blood Money

Types of Property for Diyah: Abu Hanifa vs. the Two Companions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on permissible diyah property types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on permissible diyah property types and identify the relied-upon view.

Core Idea
The relied-upon Hanafi view allows diyah from cows, sheep, and cloth, not just gold and silver.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the types of property from which diyah (blood money) may be paid. Imam Abu Hanifa held that diyah is restricted to gold and silver (or their equivalent), while his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, expanded the list to include cows, sheep, and suits of cloth. The later Hanafi tradition favoured the companions' view for its practical facilitation. By the end, you will understand the positions and why the school adopted the broader view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
First, state clearly what each party holds: Abu Hanifa's restriction vs. the Sahibayn's expansion. Note the specific items: gold, silver, cows, sheep, cloth.
The source explicitly mentions the three types according to Abu Hanifa and the additional types according to the two companions.
2
Examine the reasoning behind each view
Consider why each side might argue as they did. Abu Hanifa likely views diyah as financial compensation, best paid in currency. The companions consider custom and ease, allowing goods readily available.
The text does not provide direct dala'il, but the reasoning can be inferred from usul al-fiqh principles like istihsan and 'urf.
3
Identify the tarjih and its authority
The text states 'huwa al-mukhtār' (it is the chosen view), indicating that the school prefers the companions' position. Understand that this does not dismiss Abu Hanifa's view but gives operational weight to the other.
The tarjih is based on practical ease and is documented in later authoritative works like al-Nihayah.
4
Apply the modern interpretation
The source mentions that in later times, the 'hullah' (suit of cloth) is interpreted as a shirt and trousers. This shows how classical rulings adapt to contemporary contexts.
This interpretation from al-Nihayah demonstrates the living nature of Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تكون الدية إلا من هذه الأنواع الثلاثة عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: تجب منها ومن البقر مائتا بقرة، ومن الغنم ألفا شاة، ومن الحلل مائتا حلة كل حلة ثوبان إزار ورداء هو المختار، وفي "النهاية": قيل: في زماننا قميص وسراويل رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٨/٢]
Diyah is not (payable) except from these three types according to Abu Hanifa, and according to them (the two companions): it is obligatory from them and from cows: two hundred cows, and from sheep: two thousand sheep, and from suits of cloth: two hundred suits, each suit being two garments: an izār and a ridā' — that is the chosen (view). And in al-Nihāyah: it was said: in our time, a shirt and trousers. [Reference: Ramz al-Haqā'iq 2/438]
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi legal dispute (ikhtilāf) regarding the types of property from which diyah (blood money) may be paid. The text explains the differing views between Imam Abū Hanīfah and his two companions, Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad al-Shaybānī. Abū Hanīfah restricted the payment of diyah to three types: gold (1,000 dīnārs), silver (10,000 dirhams), or their equivalent value. The two companions expanded the permissible items to include 200 cows, 2,000 sheep, and 200 suits of cloth (hullah), each suit consisting of two garments: an izār (lower wrap) and a ridā' (upper wrap). The relied-upon position (al-mukhtār) is that of the two companions, as the text states 'huwa al-mukhtār'. Later, in al-Nihāyah, the suits of cloth were interpreted as a shirt and trousers in contemporary practice. This ruling demonstrates the flexibility within the Hanafi school in matters of compensation, aiming to facilitate payment according to available resources.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money; compensation paid to the heirs of a victim of homicide or for injury to limbs.
حلة (hullah): A suit of cloth, traditionally consisting of two garments: an izār (lower wrap) and a ridā' (upper wrap).
المختار (al-mukhtār): The chosen or relied-upon position within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only cows and sheep
D) Only gold

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position permits diyah from gold, silver, cows, sheep, and cloth, facilitating payment according to available resources.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate

Expiation for Accidental and Quasi-Intentional Killing
Ruling with proof and conditions for kaffārah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on kaffārah for accidental and quasi-intentional killing, identify the Quranic proof, and apply the conditions for a valid slave.

Core Idea
The expiation for accidental and quasi-intentional killing is identical: freeing a believing slave or fasting two months.

This lesson examines the ruling of kaffārah (expiation) for two types of killing: accidental (khaṭa') and quasi-intentional (shibh al-'amd). The passage establishes that both are subject to the same expiation as specified in Qur'an 4:92, and clarifies why feeding the poor is not an alternative. It also discusses the validity of using a suckling infant as a slave for expiation, distinguishing between outward sufficiency for expiation and the need for certainty for liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is accidental or quasi-intentional killing. The ruling is that the kaffārah is wājib (obligatory). Note that the passage equates quasi-intentional killing with accidental in terms of expiation.
Both types share the same kaffārah despite different diyah rules.
2
Trace the dalīl (proof)
The proof is Qur'an 4:92, which commands freeing a believing slave or fasting two consecutive months if unable. The passage explains that although the verse mentions accidental killing, it applies to quasi-intentional by analogy because the unintended death is similar.
The verse is explicit but the extension to quasi-intentional is based on the common element of unintended death.
3
Identify what is excluded from the expiation
Feeding the poor is not an option because it is not mentioned in the text; amounts and types of acts are only known through transmission. A fetus is not acceptable because its life and soundness are unknown.
Only two options exist: freeing a slave or fasting.
4
Analyze the condition for a valid slave: the suckling infant
A suckling infant (raḍī') is valid for expiation if one parent is Muslim, because the child follows the parent in religion and its limbs are outwardly intact. The outward appearance suffices for fulfilling the obligation, unlike liability (damān) where certainty is required.
This distinction shows Hanafi reliance on ẓāhir (outward) for fulfilling duties versus imposing penalties.
5
Apply the ruling to a hypothetical
Consider a person who accidentally kills someone. They must first try to free a believing slave; if unable, they must fast two consecutive months. Feeding is not valid. If they free a suckling infant whose father is Muslim, it is accepted.
Practical application of the sequence and conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكفارتهما أي كفارة القتل خطأ، وشبه العمد هو الذي ذكر في القرآن وهو الإعتاق والصوم على الترتيب متتابعا لما ذكر في النص، وهو وإن كان واردا في الخطأ إلا أن شبه العمد خطأ في حق القتل، وإن كان عمدا في حق الضرب، فتتناولهما الآية، ولا يختلفان فيه لعدم النقل بالاختلاف، بخلاف الدية حيث يجب في شبه العمد مغلظة لوجود التوقيف في شبه العمد دون الخطأ، والمقادير لا تجب إلا سماعا. تكملة فتح النص: وهي: ﴿وَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ فَمَنْ لَمْ يَجِدْ فَصِيَامُ شَهْرَيْنِ مُتَتَابِعَيْنِ ﴾ (الآية) (النساء:۹۲) ولا يجوز الإطعام إلخ لأن الإطعام لم يرد به النص، والمقادير لم تعرف إلا سماعا؛ ولأن المذكور كل الواجب إما في الجواب، أو لكونه كل المذكور والجنين لم تعرف حياته ولا سلامته، فلا يجوز؛ ولأنه عضو من وجه، فلا يدخل تحت مطلق النص. تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۲۹/۹] ويجوز الرضيع إلخ يعني يجوز الرضيع في الكفارة لو كان أحد أبويه مسلما؛ لأنه مسلم تبعا ،له والظاهر سلامة أطرافه على ما عليه الجبلة لا يقال كيف اكتفى هنا بالظاهر في سلامة أطرافه حتى جاز التكفير به، ولم يكتف بذلك في حق وجوب الضمان بإتلاف أطرافه؛ لأنا نقول: الحاجة في التكفير إلى دفع الواجب، والظاهر يصلح حجة للدفع، والحاجة في الإتلاف إلى إلزام الضمان، وهو لا يصلح حجة فيه؛ ولأنه يظهر حال الأطراف فيما بعد التكفير إذا عاش ولا كذلك في الإتلاف، فافترقا. (فتح)
And their expiation (kaffārah) – meaning the expiation for accidental killing (qatl khaṭa’) and quasi-intentional killing (shibh ‘amd) – is that which is mentioned in the Qur’an, namely: freeing a slave and fasting consecutively in order, as mentioned in the text. And although it is mentioned in the context of accidental killing, quasi-intentional killing is accidental in terms of killing, though it is intentional in terms of the act of hitting, so the verse covers both. They do not differ in this due to the absence of transmitted difference, unlike the diyah where it is aggravated in quasi-intentional killing because there is a transmitted ruling for that in quasi-intentional but not in accidental killing. Amounts are only known through transmission. Supplement from Fatḥ al-Qadīr: And it is: {And the freeing of a believing slave; and whoever does not find (one), then fasting for two consecutive months} (Qur’an 4:92). Feeding is not permissible because feeding is not mentioned in the text, and amounts are only known through transmission; and because what is mentioned is all that is obligatory either in response, or because it is all that is mentioned; and the fetūs (jīnīn) is not known to be alive or intact, so it is not permissible; and it is like a limb in one respect, so it does not fall under the absolute text. Supplement from al-Baḥr al-Rā’iq: And a suckling infant is permissible, i.e., a suckling infant is permissible in expiation if one of his parents is Muslim, because he is Muslim by following him, and outwardly his limbs are intact as per innate disposition. It cannot be argued how here the outward appearance was sufficient in the integrity of his limbs to allow expiation with him, while it was not sufficient in the obligation of compensation for damaging his limbs. Because we say: the need in expiation is to fulfill the obligation, and the outward appearance is a valid proof for fulfillment; while the need in damage is to impose liability, and it is not a valid proof for that; and because the condition of the limbs will become manifest after expiation if he lives, unlike in damage, so they differ. (Fatḥ)
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on the expiation (kaffārah) for accidental (khaṭa’) and quasi-intentional (shibh al-‘amd) killing. It begins by establishing that the expiation for both types is identical: freeing a believing slave or, if unable, fasting two consecutive months, as stated in Qur’an 4:92. The text then explains why feeding the poor is not an option: it is not mentioned in the Qur’an for this expiation, and the amounts and types of expiation are only known through transmission. A fetus (jīnīn) is also not acceptable because its life and physical integrity are unknown, and it is only like a limb in some respects. The passage then permits a suckling infant for expiation, under the condition that one of its parents is Muslim, because the child follows the parent in religion and its limbs are outwardly intact. The text concludes with a distinction: for expiation, the outward appearance suffices because the need is to fulfill the obligation; for liability (damān), certainty is required. This ruling highlights the Hanafi approach to textual evidence and legal reasoning.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for a sin or wrongful act, specifically for accidental or quasi-intentional killing.
شبه العمد (shibh al-‘amd): Quasi-intentional killing: the act is intentional (e.g., hitting) but the intent to kill is absent; death occurs unexpectedly.
رضيع (raḍī‘): A suckling infant, considered a valid slave for the purpose of kaffārah if one parent is Muslim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is permissible but less reward

Key Takeaway
The expiation for accidental and quasi-intentional killing is exclusively freeing a believing slave or fasting two consecutive months, with feeding and fetus excluded, and a suckling infant of one Muslim parent is valid.

Woman's Diyah and the Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Limb Compensation
Inter-madhhab dispute on woman's limb diyah and the Shafi'i one-third threshold

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the half-diyah rule for women's limbs and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the absurdity argument.

Core Idea
The woman’s diyah is half the man’s in all cases; the Shafi‘i exception leads to the absurdity that a greater injury yields less compensation.

In Hanafi fiqh, the rule that a woman’s diyah is half of a man’s is applied uniformly to all bodily injuries. Al-Shāfi‘ī, however, introduced a threshold based on one-third of the full diyah, arguing that only injuries exceeding that threshold are halved. This lesson examines the dispute and the Hanafi refutation that exposes a logical absurdity in the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that a woman's diyah is half of a man's for both soul (full diyah) and all limbs, without exception. The proof includes a narration from ‘Alī (marfū‘ and mawqūf).
This is the default rule based on general principles.
2
Identify the Shafi'i rule
Al-Shāfi‘ī distinguishes based on the amount: if the limb's diyah is one-third or less of the full diyah, it is not halved; if above one-third, it is halved. For example, a woman's finger (10 camels for a man) is not halved, so she gets 10; for three fingers (30 camels) the total is below one-third of 100, so no halving → 30; for four fingers (40) above one-third, halving gives 20.
The threshold is based on one-third of the full diyah (100 camels).
3
Examine the practical consequences
Apply both rules to the same injury scenario. For a woman's finger: Hanafi gives 5 camels (half of 10), Shafi'i gives 10. For three fingers: Hanafi gives 15, Shafi'i gives 30. For four fingers: Hanafi gives 20, Shafi'i gives 20. The Shafi'i rule produces a reversal: cutting four fingers yields less than cutting three.
4
Analyze the Hanafi critique
Al-Zayla‘ī points out that the Shafi'i position leads to a logical absurdity (muḥāl): a more severe injury (cutting four fingers) results in less compensation (20 camels) than a less severe injury (cutting three fingers, 30 camels). This contradicts the wisdom of the Lawgiver, which prescribes that greater harm deserves greater compensation.
This is a dalīl ‘aqlī (rational proof) against the Shafi'i view.
5
Conclude with tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon because it is consistent with general principles and avoids contradiction. The Shafi'i exception is not supported by strong enough evidence to override the default rule.
The later Hanafi consensus (muftā bihī) follows the universal half-rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودية المرأة إلخ حتى يجب في قتل المرأة خمسة آلاف درهم، وفي قطع يدها ألفان وخمس مائة، روي ذلك علي له موقوفا ومرفوعا وقال الشافعي: الثلث وما دون الثلث لا يتنصف، وما فوق الثلث يتنصف، فعنده يجب في قطع إصبع امرأة عشرة من الإبل، وفي إصبعين عشرون وفي ثلاثة أصابع ثلاثون، وفي أربعة عشرون بالتنصيف فيما هو أكثر الثلث من ورده الزيلعي بأن ما قال الشافعي يؤدي إلى المحال، وهو ما إذا كان ألمها أشد، ومصابها أكثر أن يقل أرشها، فإنه إذا قطع ثلاثة يجب ثلاثون ولو قطع أربعة يجب عشرون بالتنصيف فيما هو أكثر من من الثلث، فقطع الرابعة لا يوجب شيئا، بل يسقط ما وجب بقطع الثالثة، وحكمة الشارع تنافي ذلك؛ إذ . الجناية شيئا شرعا، وأقبح منه أن تسقط ما وجب لغيرها. (فتح، ملا مسكين بزيادة وتوضيح) المحال
And the diyah of a woman is half, so that in killing a woman, five thousand dirhams are obligatory, and in cutting her hand, two thousand five hundred. That is related from ‘Alī as a mawqūf and marfū‘. Al-Shāfi‘ī said: For one-third and below one-third, it is not halved, and for above one-third, it is halved. So in his view, cutting a woman’s finger requires ten camels, two fingers twenty, three fingers thirty, and four fingers twenty, by halving what is more than one-third. Al-Zayla‘ī refuted this by stating that al-Shāfi‘ī’s position leads to an absurdity: where the pain is more severe and the injury greater, the compensation becomes less. Since cutting three fingers obligates thirty, and cutting four only obligates twenty by halving what is more than one-third, cutting the fourth finger does not obligate anything; rather, it cancels what was obligated by cutting the third. The wisdom of the Lawgiver is contrary to that. It is even worse that it cancels what was obligated by another injury. (Fatḥ, Mullā Miskīn with addition and clarification) The absurdity.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and al-Shāfi‘ī concerning the application of the half-diyah rule to a woman’s limbs. The Hanafi position, based on a narration from ‘Alī, is that the diyah of a woman is half that of a man in all cases, including limbs. Thus, for killing a woman, the diyah is 5,000 dirhams (half of 10,000), and for cutting a hand, it is 2,500 dirhams. Al-Shāfi‘ī, however, differentiates based on the proportion of the diyah : if the limb’s diyah is one-third or less of the full diyah, it is not halved; if it exceeds one-third, it is halved. Thus, for a woman’s finger (which would be 10 camels for a man, i.e., one-third of the full diyah of 30 camels for a hand?), he gives 10 camels (no halving), but for three fingers (30 camels, more than one-third), he halved to 15? Wait, the text says: for three fingers, he gives thirty? Actually, the text says: 'In his view, cutting a woman’s finger requires ten camels, two fingers twenty, three fingers thirty, and four fingers twenty.' This indicates that for four fingers, which is above one-third of 40? He applies halving only to the amount above one-third. The Hanafi commentator al-Zayla‘ī refutes this by showing it leads to an absurdity: cutting three fingers obligates 30 camels, but cutting four (which is more severe) only 20, meaning the fourth finger actually reduces the compensation. This violates the principle that greater injury should incur greater compensation. The Hanafi position is thus consistent with legal rationality (qa‘ida).

Key Terms
تنصيص (tansīṣ): Halving, reduction to half; here referring to the rule that a woman's diyah is half of a man's.
الثلث (al-thulth): One-third; a threshold used by Shafi'i to determine whether the half rule applies to a woman's limb diyah.
المحال (al-muhāl): Absurdity, impossibility; used to describe the logical conclusion of the opposing school's position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 50
C) 12.5
D) 10

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school applies the half-diyah rule for women uniformly to all limbs, and the Shafi'i exception is refuted by the absurdity of greater injury leading to lesser compensation.

Equality of Diyah between Muslim and Dhimmi
Inter-madhhab dispute on diyah equality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the diyah of a dhimmi is equal to that of a Muslim and evaluate the opposing evidences from Malik and Shafi'i.

Core Idea
The diyah of a dhimmi equals a Muslim's because deficiency in diyah is due to lack of ownership, not inherent inferiority.

This lesson examines a critical inter-madhhab dispute regarding blood money (diyah) for homicide when the victim is a non-Muslim citizen (dhimmi) or one under safe passage (musta'min). While the Hanafi school affirms equality, other schools reduce the amount. Why? The text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents the proofs and the rational argument that diyah reduction for women and slaves is based on ownership capacity, not status.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its proof
Identify the ruling: diyah of a Muslim, dhimmi, and musta'min are equal for the soul. Cite the proofs: hadith of Ibn Abbas and statement of Ali.
The hadith shows the Prophet assessed 100 camels for two dhimmis, equal to a Muslim's diyah.
2
Identify opposing positions
Note Malik's view (half diyah) and Shafi'i's old view (one-third for Jews/Christians, less for Magians). List their proofs.
Malik relies on a report from Ali; Shafi'i on other narrations.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the rational objection
Understand the objection that kufr is a greater deficiency than femininity or slavery, so diyah should be even lower. Then see the Hanafi refutation: reduction for women/slaves is due to lack of ownership (maliyah), not inherent status; a free kafir has full ownership, so equality.
The key distinction: the cause of reduction is ownership capacity, not inherent worth (like religion).
4
Determine the practical difference
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: if a Muslim kills a dhimmi, Hanafi requires full diyah (1000 dinars), Malik requires half (6000 dirhams), Shafi'i old requires less (4000 dirhams).
This affects actual compensation in legal cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودية المسلم والذمي سواء في حق النفس وإن كان مجوسيا وكذا المستأمن. قال مالك: دية اليهودي والنصراني ستة آلاف درهم؛ لقوله علي: عقل الكافر نصف دية المسلم، والكل عنده اثنا عشر ألفا، وقال الشافعي دية النصراني واليهودي أربعة آلاف درهم، ودية المجوسي ثمان مائة درهم؛ لأنه روي هكذا، وهذا على قوله القديم وبه قال أحمد ومالك في رواية وعلى قوله الجديد: ثلث الإبل، أو قيمة الثلث عند فقدها، وكذلك في المجوسي. ولنا: ما روي عن ابن عباس لما أن النبي هودى المعاهدين الذين كان لهما عهد من رسول الله ، وقتلهما عمرو بن أمية الضمري بمائة من الإبل، وقال علي: "دية كل ذي عهد في عهده ألف دينار"، وعن الزهري أن أبا بكر ما كانا يجعلان دية الذمي مثل دية المسلم. رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٩/۲] ولا يقال: إن نقص الكفر فوق نقص الأنوثة والرق فوجب أن تنتقص ديته به كما تنتقص بالأنوثة والرق، ولأن الرق أثر الكفر ، فإذا انتقص بأثره فأولى أن ينتقص به ؛ لأنا نقول : نقصان دية المرأة والعبد لا باعتبار نقصان الأنوثة والرق، بل باعتبار نقصان صفة المالكية، فإن المرأة لا تملك بالنكاح، والعبد لا يملك المال والحر الذكر يملكهما، فلهذا زادت قيمته ونقصت قيمتهما، والكافر يساوي المسلم في هذا المعنى فوجب أن يكون بدله كبدله، والمستأمن ديته مثل دية الذمي. (فتح، مسكين)
And the diyah of a Muslim and a dhimmi are equal in respect to the soul, even if he is a Magian, and likewise the musta'min. Mālik said: The diyah of a Jew and a Christian is six thousand dirhams, based on the statement of ‘Alī: The 'aql of the kāfir is half the diyah of a Muslim, and in his view the full diyah is twelve thousand. Al-Shāfi‘ī said: The diyah of a Christian and a Jew is four thousand dirhams, and of a Magian is eight hundred dirhams, because it is thus narrated. This is his old view, and Aḥmad and Mālik in one narration also said this. In his new view, it is one-third of the camels, or the value of one-third if camels are unavailable, and likewise for the Magian. Our proof: What has been narrated from Ibn ‘Abbās that the Prophet (ﷺ) gave a ruling of diyah for two protected persons (mu‘āhidān) who had a covenant from the Messenger of Allāh, and ‘Amr ibn Umayyah al-Ḍamrī killed them, as one hundred camels. And ‘Alī said: 'The diyah of every covenant-holder under his covenant is one thousand dīnārs.' And from al-Zuhrī that Abū Bakr and ‘Umar used to make the diyah of the dhimmi equal to that of the Muslim. [Reference: Ramz al-Ḥaqā’iq 2/439] And it cannot be said: The deficiency of disbelief is above the deficiency of femininity and slavery, so it is obligatory that his diyah be reduced accordingly, just as it is reduced for femininity and slavery, and because slavery is an effect of disbelief, so if it is reduced by its effect, it is more appropriate that it be reduced by the cause itself. Because we say: The reduction of the diyah of a woman and a slave is not due to the deficiency of femininity and slavery, but due to the deficiency of the quality of ownership (mālikīyah). For a woman does not own through marriage, and a slave does not own property, while a free male owns them both. For this reason, his value increases and their value decreases. The disbeliever (kāfir) equals the Muslim in this sense, so it is obligatory that his blood money be like his blood money. And the musta'min's diyah is like that of the dhimmi. (Fatḥ, Miskīn)
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the diyah (blood money) for homicide of a Muslim versus a dhimmi (non-Muslim citizen) or musta'min (non-Muslim under safe passage). The Hanafi position, which is the subject of this text, holds that the diyah for the soul (nafs) is equal for all: Muslim, dhimmi (including Magians), and musta'min. The text then cites the opposing views: Mālik holds that the diyah of a Jew or Christian is half of a Muslim's (6,000 out of 12,000 dirhams). Al-Shāfi‘ī (in his old view) holds that the diyah of a Jew/Christian is 4,000 dirhams and of a Magian is 800 dirhams; in his new view, it is one-third of the camels. The Hanafi proof includes a narration from Ibn ‘Abbās that the Prophet (ﷺ) assessed 100 camels for two protected persons who were killed, and a statement from ‘Alī that the diyah of every covenant-holder is 1,000 dīnārs. Also, the practice of Abū Bakr and ‘Umar is cited. The text then refutes a potential objection: if it is argued that the deficiency of disbelief (kufr) is greater than that of femininity or slavery, so the diyah of a kāfir should be reduced just as for a woman or slave, and even more so because slavery is a consequence of disbelief; the Hanafi response is that the reduction for women and slaves is not due to inherent deficiency but due to their lack of full ownership (mālīyah). A woman does not own property through marriage (i.e., she cannot own the dower received in marriage? Or more generally, her ownership capacity is limited? Actually, the text says 'al-mar'ah lā tamlik bi al-nikāḥ' meaning a woman does not own by marriage? That seems odd. The idea is that her capacity to own and earn is less than a free male, and a slave does not own property at all. A free male owns both, so his value is higher. A disbeliever, however, has the same ownership capacity as a Muslim, so his diyah should be equal. Therefore, the Hanafi position is that diyah is equal. The musta'min's diyah is like that of a dhimmi.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim citizen living permanently in an Islamic state under a covenant (dhimmah) guaranteeing protection.
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters the Islamic lands with a safe passage (amān).
معاهد (mu‘āhid): A non-Muslim who has a treaty or covenant (ʿahd) with the Muslim state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely holds that diyah for the soul is equal regardless of religion, based on ownership capacity rather than inherent worth.

General Principles of Limb Diyah and Specifics for Nose and Tongue
Ruling with proof on conditions for full diyah and specifics for nose and tongue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when full diyah is due on a limb, identify the exceptions where only ḥukūmat 'adl applies, and calculate apportionment for partial tongue loss.

Core Idea
Full diyah is due only when a complete benefit or beauty is lost; otherwise, equitable compensation applies.

In this lesson, we explore the classical Hanafi rule for diyah (blood money) on limbs. The text establishes a key principle: full diyah is obligatory only when the injury destroys a complete benefit or a complete intended beauty. It then applies this principle to the nose and tongue, providing detailed rulings on partial loss and apportionment. By understanding this principle, you will be able to distinguish between cases requiring full diyah and those requiring only equitable compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Read the passage and find the explicit statement of the rule: if one destroys a type of benefit completely or eliminates a intended beauty completely, full diyah is due. Note the qualification 'complete' (الكمال).
The rule is derived from the statement of ‘Alī (ra) and applied by analogy to other limbs.
2
Locate the Dalil
The passage cites the statement of ‘Alī: 'For the tongue, full diyah; for the nose, full diyah.' This is the textual evidence. Understand that this text is used by analogy (dalālah) for other limbs that share the same meaning.
The dalil is a Hadith of a Companion, considered zannī al-thubūt and zannī al-dalālah.
3
Apply the Rule to the Nose (Mārun)
The passage defines the mārun as the soft fleshy part of the nose. Cutting it off requires full diyah. If cut from the bone, the diyah is still only one full diyah, not increased.
This is a specific application of the general rule: the nose provides both benefit (breathing) and beauty.
4
Apply the Rule to the Tongue (Lisān)
The tongue's primary benefit is speech. Full diyah is due for its complete loss. However, if the tongue is lost but the person could not speak (e.g., a mute), only ḥukūmat 'adl is due. For partial loss, the diyah is apportioned according to the letters lost.
There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abū Yūsuf may have a different view, but the ẓāhir al-riwāyah is that full diyah is due.
5
Identify Exceptions (Cases of Incomplete Benefit or Beauty)
The passage lists examples where full diyah is not due: cutting the tongue of a mute, testicles of a eunuch, a flaccid hand, etc. In these cases, only equitable compensation (ḥukūmat 'adl) is required because the benefit or beauty was already incomplete.
This is the key distinction: the loss must be of a complete function or appearance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني في هذه الأشياء المذكورة من النفس والمارن واللسان والذكر وغيرها الدية، والأصل فيه: قوله علي: "في اللسان الدية، وفي المارن الدية"، فالنص الوارد في البعض يكون واردا في الباقي دلالة؛ لأنه في معناه والأصل في الأعضاء: أنه إذا فوت جنس منفعة على وجه الكمال أو أزال جمالا مقصودا في الآدمي على الكمال، تجب الدية؛ لأن فيه إتلاف النفس من وجه؛ إذ النفس لا تبقي منتفعا بها ذلك الوجه، وإنما قيدنا بالكمال للاحتراز عن غير الكامل حيث لا يجب فيه كل من الدية، وإن كان فيه تفويت عضو مقصود كما إذا قطع لسان الأخرس، أو آلة الخصي أو العنين أو اليد الشلاء أو الرجل العرجاء أو العين العوراء أو السن السوداء لا يجب القصاص في العمد، ولا الدية في الخطأ؛ لأنه لم يفوت منفعة، ولا فوت جمالا على الكمال، وإنما فيه حكومة عدل، فافهم واحفظ. (فتح بتغيير وتوضيح) والمارن وهو مالان من الأنف، وفضل عن القصبة رمز الحقائق: ٤٤٠/٢] ففي المارن الدية، وكذا لو قطعه من القصبة لا يزيد على دية واحدة. (فتح) واللسان عمدا كان إتلاف اللسان والذكر أو خطأ في ظاهر الرواية وقد مر خلاف أبي يوسف فيه لفوات منفعة مقصودة، وهو النطق، وكذا في قطع بعضه إذا امتنع عن الكلام، ولو قدر على التكلم ببعض الحروف دون البعض، تقسم الدية على عدد الحروف، وقيل على عدد حروف تتعلق باللسان .
It means that for these mentioned things – the soul, the nose, the tongue, the penis, and others – there is full diyah. The basis for this is the statement of ‘Alī: 'For the tongue, the full diyah; for the nose, the full diyah.' So the text that is mentions in some cases applies to the rest by implication, because they are in its meaning. And the rule regarding limbs is that if one destroys a type of benefit completely or eliminates a intended beauty in a human being completely, full diyah is obligatory, because it is in a way destruction of the soul, since the soul is not left benefitting from that aspect. We restricted it to 'complete' to exclude incomplete cases where full diyah is not obligatory, even if it involves the loss of a intended limb, such as if one cuts the tongue of a mute, or the testicles of a eunuch or an impotent man, or a flaccid hand, or a lame leg, or a blind eye, or a black tooth – neither qiṣāṣ is obligatory for intentional injury nor full diyah for accidental injury, because it did not destroy benefit nor did it eliminate beauty completely; there is instead an equitable compensation (ḥukūmat ‘adl). So understand and memorize. (Fatḥ with change and clarification) And the mārun is the soft part of the nose, and it is additional to the bone. [Reference: Ramz al-Ḥaqā’iq 2/440] So for the mārun, full diyah. Likewise, if he cuts it from the bone, it does not exceed a single diyah. (Fatḥ) And the tongue – whether destroying the tongue or the penis is intentional or accidental in the apparent narration, and the difference with Abū Yūsuf has passed regarding it, due to the loss of a intended benefit, which is speech. And likewise, if he cuts part of it and he refrains from speaking, or if he is able to speak some letters but not others, the diyah is divided according to the number of letters. And it is said: according to the number of letters related to the tongue.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage lays down the general principle for diyah (blood money) for limbs and then applies it to the nose (mārun) and tongue. The text begins by noting that full diyah is due for the limbs mentioned (soul, nose, tongue, etc.) based on a statement of ‘Alī: 'For the tongue, full diyah; for the nose, full diyah.' This text is applied to other limbs by analogy (dalālah) because they share the same meaning. The general rule is: if an injury destroys a complete benefit (manfa‘ah) or eliminates a intended beauty (jamāl) in a human being completely, full diyah is obligatory. This is because such injury effectively destroys part of the soul's utility. Any incompleteness in the benefit or beauty excludes full diyah. For example, cutting the tongue of a mute, or the testicles of a eunuch, or a flaccid hand, etc., does not attract full diyah but only equitable compensation (ḥukūmat ‘adl). The text then specifies the nose: the mārun is the soft part of the nose; if it is cut off, full diyah is due. Cutting it from the bone does not increase the diyah beyond one full diyah. For the tongue, full diyah is due if it is completely destroyed, whether intentionally or accidentally according to the apparent narration (ẓāhir al-riwāyah), though there is a different view from Abū Yūsuf. If part of the tongue is cut and speech is completely lost, full diyah is due. If the person can speak some letters but not others, the diyah is divided proportionally according to the number of letters. Some say it is divided according to the number of letters related to the tongue.

Key Terms
مارن (mārun): The soft, fleshy part of the nose, excluding the bony bridge (qaṣabah).
لسان (lisān): Tongue; full diyah is due for complete loss of speech function.
حكومة عدل (ḥukūmat ‘adl): An equitable compensation assessed by a judge based on the value of the lost part relative to the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following injuries would NOT require full diyah?
A) Cutting off the entire nose (mārun) of a person with a normal nose
B) Cutting off the tongue of a person who was born completely mute
C) Cutting off the tongue of a person who could speak normally
D) Cutting off the ear of a person with perfect hearing and normal appearance

Key Takeaway
Full diyah for a limb is only obligatory when it destroys a complete benefit or beauty; otherwise, hukumat 'adl applies.
