Definition and Scope of Shijaj (Head Wounds)
Defines shijaj, its scope, and enumerates ten types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define shajjah, explain why fixed diyat applies only to head/face wounds, and list the ten types of shijaj in order of increasing severity.

Core Idea
A shajjah is a head/face wound; fixed diyat applies only here; elsewhere it's hukumat 'adl.

The Hanafi school devotes a separate chapter to shijaj because of their many sub-types. This passage clarifies that the term shajjah is linguistically and legally restricted to wounds on the head or face. For wounds elsewhere, the fixed diyat amounts do not apply; instead, a just compensation (hukumat 'adl) is assessed. The passage then provides a systematic binary classification leading to ten distinct types, which form the basis for varying diyat amounts.

Guided Path
1
Define shajjah linguistically and legally
Identify the linguistic meaning: any wound on head or face. Confirm the legal usage matches this, so that a wound elsewhere is called jurh, not shajjah. Understand that the ruling (fixed diyat) is tied to this definition.
The author explains: 'al-shajjah fi al-lughah hua al-jarh alladhi yakun fi al-wajh aw al-ra's.'
2
Identify the scope of fixed diyat
Fixed diyat amounts are based on prophetic tradition (tawqif) and are limited to what has been transmitted. Since the Prophet (SAW) only fixed amounts for head/face wounds, those are the only ones with arsh muqaddar. For any other wound, the judge must assess hukumat 'adl.
Exception: ja'ifah (chest wound) also has a fixed amount, as per al-Zahirah.
3
Trace the binary classification
The text uses a binary tree: cut skin only or deeper. For skin only: blood appears (harisah if no blood, damiyah/dami'ah if blood). For deeper: cut flesh (badi'ah) or membrane (simhaq), etc. Follow each branch to derive the ten types.
The classification is: 1. harisah, 2. damiyah, 3. dami'ah, 4. badi'ah, 5. mutlahimah, 6. simhaq, 7. mudihah, 8. hashimah, 9. naqilah, 10. ammah.
4
List the ten types in order
Memorize the sequence: from superficial to severe. Note that hashimah was omitted in some transmissions but is essential to complete the ten. Use the binary divisions to reconstruct the list if forgotten.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: عقد للشجاج فصلا على حدة لكثرة مسائلها، وإن كانت نوعا من أنواع ما دون النفس. (فتح) في الشجاج: الشجاج - بالكسر - جمع شجة بالفتح، وهي في اللغة الجرح يكون في الوجه أو الرأس، وما يكون بغيرهما يسمى جراحة، فالحكم مرتب على الحقيقة أي حكم الشجاج يثبت في الوجه والرأس على ما هو حقيقة اللغة؛ لأن الشجة لغة ما كان فيهما لا غير، وفي غيرهما لا يجب المقدر فيهما، بل يجب حكومة عدل، فلو تحققت الموضحة مثلا في نحو الساق واليد لا يجب الأرش المقدر لها؛ لأنها جراحة لا موضحة، بل يجب حكومة عدل؛ لأن التقدير بالتوقيف، وهذا إنما ورد في ما يختص بالوجه والرأس، ولا شيء من الجراح لها أرش معلوم إلا الجائفة كما في "الظهيرية". واعلم أن الشجاج عشرة؛ لأن الشجة إما أن تقطع الجلد فقط، أو تزيد عليه، والأولى إما أن يظهر فيها دم أو لا، الأولى الحارصة، والثانية إما أن تسيل أو لا، الثاني: الدامعة والأول: الدامية، وما يزيد إما أن يقطع اللحم الذي بينه وبين العظم أو لا، الثاني: الباضعة والأول: إما أن يظهر القطع الجلدة الرقيقة الحائلة بين الجلد والعظم أو لا، الثاني: المتلاحمة والأولى السمحاق. ثم إن أظهرت العظم ولم تنقله، فهي الموضحة وإن نقلت فهي المنقلة إن لم تصل إلى الجلدة التي بين العظم والدماغ، وإن وصلت فهي الآمة، هكذا نقل في "الفتح" عن "الحموي"، وقد ترك فيه ذكر العاشرة، وهي الهاشمة، فاحفظه، فهذه عشر شجاج، وقد ذكر المصنف أحكامها مفصلا، فقال: في الموضحة إلخ.
Chapter: He devoted a separate section to shijaj due to the abundance of its issues, although they are a type of the types below homicide. (Fath) On shijaj: Shijaj (with kasr) is the plural of shajjah (with fatha), which linguistically is a wound on the face or head. What occurs elsewhere is called a jurh (wound). So the ruling is based on the literal meaning: the ruling of shijaj is confirmed on the face and head as per the linguistic reality, because shajjah linguistically is only what is in these two. Elsewhere, the fixed compensation is not obligatory; rather, just compensation (hukumat 'adl) is obligatory. For example, if a mudihah occurred on the leg or hand, the fixed arsh is not obligatory because it is a wound, not a mudihah; rather, just compensation is obligatory because the fixed amounts are based on prophetic tradition (tawqif), and this is only reported for what is specific to the face and head. No wound has a known arsh except the ja'ifah, as in al-Zahirah. Know that shijaj are ten: because the shajjah either cuts the skin only or goes deeper. The first: either blood appears or not—the first is harisah. The second: either it flows or not—the second is dami'ah and the first is damiyah. What increases: either it cuts the flesh between it and the bone, or not—the second is badi'ah and the first: either the thin membrane between the skin and bone appears cut or not—the second is mutlahimah and the first is simhaq. Then if it exposes the bone without moving it, it is mudihah; if it moves it, it is naqilah if it does not reach the membrane between the bone and the brain; if it reaches it, it is ammah. Thus it is transmitted in al-Fath from al-Hamawi, and he omitted the tenth, which is hashimah, so remember it. So these are ten shijaj, and the author mentioned their rulings in detail, saying: 'In the mudihah... etc.'
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This is a definition and classification passage. The commentator (from al-Fath) first defines shijaj linguistically and legally: shajjah is specifically a wound on the head or face; wounds elsewhere are called jurh and do not carry fixed diyat amounts unless explicitly stated (like ja'ifah). The fixed amounts (muqaddar) are based on tawqif (prophetic determination), so they are limited to what is transmitted. The passage then classifies the ten types of shijaj using a binary division: starting from the skin, each level cuts deeper. The ten types are: 1. harisah (scratch without blood), 2. damiyah (oozing blood), 3. dami'ah (flowing blood), 4. badi'ah (cuts skin), 5. mutlahimah (cuts flesh), 6. simhaq (reaches membrane over bone), 7. mudihah (exposes bone), 8. hashimah (fractures bone), 9. naqilah (displaces bone), 10. ammah (reaches brain membrane). The author notes that hashimah was omitted in the list from al-Hamawi but is essential to complete the ten. The classification is foundational for understanding the varying diyat amounts for each type.

Key Terms
الشجة (al-shajjah): A wound on the head or face, as per linguistic and legal usage; on other body parts it is called jurh.
حكومة عدل (hukumat 'adl): Just compensation assessed by a qualified evaluator, not a fixed amount, used when no textual arsh exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A shajjah is restricted to head/face wounds; fixed diyat amounts only apply there, and elsewhere hukumat 'adl is wajib.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Fixed Diyat for Major Shijaj: Mudihah, Hashimah, Naqilah, Ma'mumah, and Ja'ifah
States fixed diyat amounts with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fixed diyat amounts for the five major head wounds (mudihah, hashimah, naqilah, ammah/ma'mumah, ja'ifah) and identify the hadith evidence that establishes each.

Core Idea
Fixed camel amounts for specific head wounds are wajib based on the Prophet's ﷺ instruction, ranging from 1/20 to 1/3 of the full diyah.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq establishes the specific diyat amounts for five major shijaj (head wounds). These fixed amounts derive from the well-known letter of 'Amr ibn Hazm, a document containing the Prophet's ﷺ rulings on diyat. Understanding these amounts is essential for correctly assessing compensation in accidental and quasi-intentional injury cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five wound types and their definitions
Read the passage to define mudihah (exposes bone), hashimah (fractures bone), naqilah (displaces bone), ammah/ma'mumah (reaches brain membrane), and ja'ifah (penetrates body cavity). Note that ja'ifah can occur in head or abdomen.
Ja'ifah is not among the ten shijaj but shares ruling with ammah.
2
State the fixed diyat amount for each wound
From the text: mudihah gets half a tenth (1/20 = 5 camels), hashimah gets one tenth (1/10 = 10 camels), naqilah gets one and a half tenths (3/20 = 15 camels), ammah and ja'ifah get one third (1/3 ≈ 33.33 camels).
These are fractions of the full diyah of 100 camels.
3
Identify the textual evidence
The hadith from the letter of 'Amr ibn Hazm is the primary evidence: 'In the mudihah, five camels; in the hashimah, ten; in the naqilah, fifteen; in the ammah, a third of the blood money.' For ja'ifah, a narration from 'Ali is also cited.
The hadith is ahad but the Hanafi school accepts it as binding due to its clarity and acceptance by the Companions.
4
Understand the scope of application
These fixed amounts apply to accidental (khata) and quasi-intentional (shibh al-'amd) injuries. If the wound is intentional, qisas (retaliation) applies instead of diyat. Also, the ja'ifah is not included among the ten shijaj because it can occur in the abdomen, but its diyat is the same as ammah.
If ja'ifah penetrates through the body, two-thirds of the full diyah are due (entry and exit wounds).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الموضحة نصف عشر الدية، وفي الهاشمة عشرها، وفي المنقلة عشر ونصف عشر، وفي الآمة أو الجائفة ثلثها التي تصل إلى الجوف. في الموضحة إلخ إن كانت خطأ، وإن كانت عمدا ففيها القصاص أي في الموضحة، وهي التي توضح العظم أي تبينه وتكشفه، نصف عشر الدية؛ لما روي في كتاب عمرو بن حزم أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: "في الموضحة خمس من الإبل، وفي الهاشمة عشر، وفي المنقلة خمس عشرة، وفي الآمة - ويروى - المأمومة ثلث الدية" [رمز الحقائق: ٢/٤٤٢] وفي الهاشمة إلخ وهي التي تهشم العظم أي تكسره عشرها أي عشر الدية، لما روينا. [رمز الحقائق: ٢/٤٤٢] وفي المنقلة إلخ: وهي التي تنقل العظم بعد الكسر فيها عشر ونصف عشر الدية لما روينا [رمز الحقائق: ٢/٤٤٢] وفي الآمة بتشديد الميم وهي التي تصل إلى أم الدماغ، وأم الدماغ هي الجلدة الرقيقة التي تجمع الدماغ. أو الجائفة ثلثها: أي ثلث الدية الجائفة: وهي التي تكون في الرأس والبطن، بخلاف سائر الشجاج حيث لا تكون إلا في الرأس والوجه، وقيل: لا تتحقق الجائفة فيما فوق الحلق، فلذلك لم تدخل الجائفة في العشرة؛ لأنها لا يطلق عليها الشجة، وإنما ذكرت مع الآمة؛ لاستوائهما في الحكم، قال علي: "في الجائفة ثلث الدية"، وكذلك في الآمة ثلث الدية لما روينا الآن. [رمز الحقائق: ٢/٤٤٢] فإن نفذت الجائفة فثلثاها
In the mudihah, half of a tenth of the blood-money; in the hashimah, its tenth; in the naqilah, a tenth and half a tenth; in the ammah or ja'ifah, its third—that which reaches the body cavity. In the mudihah etc., if it is accidental; if intentional, then retribution (qisas) applies, i.e., in the mudihah, which exposes the bone, i.e., makes it appear and uncover it: half of a tenth of the blood-money. Because it is narrated in the letter of 'Amr ibn Hazm that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'In the mudihah, five camels; in the hashimah, ten; in the naqilah, fifteen; in the ammah (also narrated as ma'mumah), a third of the blood-money.' (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:442) In the hashimah etc., which breaks the bone, its tenth, i.e., a tenth of the blood-money, as we narrated. In the naqilah etc., which moves the bone after breaking, in it a tenth and half a tenth of the blood-money, as we narrated. In the ammah (with shaddah on the mim), which reaches the membrane of the brain (umm al-dimagh) — and umm al-dimagh is the thin membrane that holds the brain — or the ja'ifah: its third, i.e., a third of the blood-money. The ja'ifah is that which occurs in the head or abdomen, unlike other shijaj which are only on the head and face. It is said: the ja'ifah does not apply above the throat; therefore the ja'ifah is not included among the ten because it is not called a shajjah; rather it is mentioned with the ammah because they are equal in ruling. 'Ali said: 'In the ja'ifah, a third of the blood-money,' and likewise in the ammah a third of the blood-money, as we have now narrated. If the ja'ifah penetrates, then two-thirds of it (i.e., two-thirds of the blood-money).
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage provides the fixed diyat amounts for the five major shijaj: mudihah (exposing bone) gets half of a tenth of the full diyah (i.e., 5 camels out of 100, or 1/20 of full diyah); hashimah (fracturing bone) gets one-tenth (10 camels); naqilah (displacing bone) gets one and a half tenths (15 camels); ammah (reaching the brain membrane) and ja'ifah (penetrating wound to the head or abdomen) each get one-third of the full diyah. The evidence is the hadith from the letter of 'Amr ibn Hazm, which is a well-known document. The commentator clarifies that ja'ifah is not counted among the ten types of shijaj because it can occur in the abdomen and is not limited to the head/face, but it shares the same ruling as ammah. Additionally, if the ja'ifah penetrates through the body (nufudh), the compensation is two-thirds of the full diyah, based on the precedent of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq. This is because it becomes two wounds: one entry and one exit. The passage also notes that for intentional mudihah, qisas applies (as will be detailed later).

Key Terms
الموضحة (al-mudihah): A wound that exposes the bone (iwdah: to make clear).
الهاشمة (al-hashimah): A wound that fractures the bone (hashm: to break).
المنقلة (al-naqilah): A wound that displaces the bone after fracture (naql: to move).
الآمة (al-ammah/ma'mumah): A wound that reaches the brain membrane (umm al-dimagh).
الجائفة (al-ja'ifah): A wound that reaches the internal cavity (jawf) of the head or abdomen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the diyat fraction for a mudihah (bone-exposing wound)?
A) 1/10
B) 1/20
C) 1/15
D) 1/3

Key Takeaway
Fixed diyat amounts for the five major head wounds are wajib based on the prophetic hadith, ranging from 1/20 (mudihah) to 1/3 (ammah/ja'ifah).

Six Minor Shijaj: Definitions and Hukumat 'Adl
Defines six minor shijaj and mandates just compensation with intra-Hanafi methodological ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six minor shijaj by their definitions, explain why each requires hukumat 'adl, and compare the three Hanafi methods for calculating just compensation.

Core Idea
Six head wounds with no fixed arsh require just compensation, calculated proportionally to the mudihah per the soundest Hanafi view.

This lesson examines the six minor shijaj (head wounds) that fall between negligible scratches and the major wound (mudihah) that has a fixed arsh. For these wounds, the law mandates just compensation (hukumat 'adl) assessed by a fair expert. We will break down the linguistic definitions of each wound, understand why no fixed amount is given, and then explore the intra-Hanafi debate on how to calculate the compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six wounds and their severity order
List the six shijaj: harisah, dami'ah, damiyah, badi'ah, mutlahimah, simhaq. Note that they are the 5th through 10th shijaj in ascending severity, with simhaq reaching the bone membrane.
The numbering is based on the classical list of ten shijaj; the four major ones (1-4) have fixed arsh.
2
Define each wound linguistically
For each wound, examine the Arabic root and how the definition connects to the physical damage. For example, harisah means 'scratched' without blood; dami'ah shows blood but does not flow; etc.
Some terms are derived from optimistic omen (mutlahimah) or from the instrument used (badi'ah).
3
Understand why no fixed arsh exists
Recognize that for these six wounds, there is no transmitted (sam'i) fixed penalty. They are not negligible (cannot be ignored) but do not reach the threshold of mudihah (which exposes bone) that has a fixed half-tenth of diyah.
Shari'ah specifies fixed amounts only for major wounds; lesser wounds require ijtihad.
4
Recognize the obligation of hukumat 'adl
State that for each of these wounds, wajib is to pay just compensation assessed by an expert. This principle is reported from early authorities like Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz.
The word 'adl in hukumat 'adl emphasizes fairness, not arbitrariness.
5
Compare the three Hanafi methods of calculation
List al-Tahawi's method (depreciation of slave value), al-Karkhi's method (proportion to mudihah), and the soundest method from al-Muhit (proportion to the least wound with fixed arsh, i.e., mudihah). Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: different methods yield different amounts.
The soundest view (al-Muhit) is considered the mufta bihi in later Hanafi fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الحارصة والدامعة والدامية والباضعة والمتلاحمة والسمحاق حكومة عدل. والحارصة هي التي تحرص الجلد أي تخدشه، ولا تخرج الدم مأخوذ من حرص القصار الثوب إذا شقه في الدق، وهي الشجة الخامسة. والدامعة بالعين المهملة هي التي تظهر الدم، ولا تسيله كالدمع في العين مأخوذة من الدمع سميت بها؛ لأن الدم يخرج منها بقدر الدمع من المقلة، وقيل: لأن عينيه تدمعان بسبب ألم يحصل له منها، وهي الشجة السادسة، الدامية وهي التي تسيل الدم، وذكر المرغيناني أن الدامية هي التي تدمي من غير أن تسيل منها هو الصحيح، مروي عن أبي عبيد وهي الشجة السابعة والباضعة وهي التي تبضع الجلد أي تقطعه مأخوذة من البضع، وهو الفصاد الشق، ومنه مبضع الشجة الثامنة، والمتلاحمة وهي التي تأخذ في اللحم فتقطعه كله، ثم يتلاحم بعد ذلك أي يلتئم ويتلاصق سميت بذلك تفاؤلا على ما تؤول إليه، وقال الأزهري: الأوجه أن يقال: اللاحمة أي القاطعة اللحم، وهي الشجة التاسعة والسمحاق وهي التي تصل إلى السمحاق: وهي الجلدة الرقيقة التي بين اللحم وعظم الرأس، وهي الشجة العاشرة. [رمز الحقائق: ٢/٤٤٢] حكومة عدل إلخ: أي في كل واحد من هذه الشجاج الست حكومة عدل؛ لأنه ليس فيها أرش مقدر من جهة السمع، ولا يمكن إهدارها، فيجب فيها حكومة عدل، وهو مأثور عن إبراهيم النخعي وعمر بن عبد العزيز حملا، واختلفوا في تفسيرها، فقال الطحاوي: هي أن يقوم مملوكا بدون هذا الأثر، ثم يقوم وبه هذا الأثر، ثم ينظر إلى تفاوت ما بينهما، فإن كان ثلث عشر القيمة مثلا يجب ثلث عشر الدية، وإن كان ربع عشر القيمة، يجب ربع عشر الدية، وقال الكرخي ينظر مقدار هذه الشجة من الموضحة، فيجب بقدر ذلك من نصف عشر الدية؛ لأن ما لا نص فيه يرد إلى المنصوص عليه، وفي "المحيط": والأصح أنه ينظر كم مقدار هذه الشجة من أقل شجة لها أرش مقدر، فإن كان مقداره مثل نصف شجة لها أرش أو ثلثها، وجب نصف أوثلث أرش تلك الشجة، وإن كان ربعها، فربع. [رمز الحقائق: ٢/ ٤٤٣]
And for al-harisah, al-dami'ah, al-damiyah, al-badi'ah, al-mutlahimah, and al-simhaq: just compensation (hukumat 'adl). Al-harisah is that which scratches the skin, i.e., scrapes it, without drawing blood, derived from the fuller tearing cloth when beating it. It is the fifth shajjah. Al-dami'ah (with an important 'ayn) is that which shows blood but does not let it flow, like tears in the eye, derived from tears; it is named so because blood comes out in the amount of a tear from the eyeball; or it is said that the eyes tear due to the pain. It is the sixth shajjah. Al-damiyah is that which makes the blood flow, but al-Marghinani mentioned that the correct view is that al-damiyah is that which bleeds without flowing, narrated from Abu 'Ubayd. It is the seventh shajjah. Al-badi'ah is that which cuts the skin, derived from bad', which is phlebotomy/incision, hence the lancet; it is the eighth shajjah. Al-mutlahimah is that which takes the flesh, cutting it entirely, then it heals (mutlahimah) — so named as an optimistic omen for its outcome. Al-Azhari said: the better expression is lahimah, i.e., the flesh-cutter. It is the ninth shajjah. Al-simhaq is that which reaches the simhaq, which is the thin membrane between the flesh and the skull. It is the tenth shajjah. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:442] Hukumat 'adl etc.: i.e., for each of these six shijaj, just compensation is required because no fixed arsh is transmitted from scripture, and they cannot be ignored, so hukumat 'adl is obligatory. This is reported from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz. They differed in its interpretation. Al-Tahawi said: a slave is valued without this effect, then valued with this effect, and the difference between the two is considered; if the depreciation is, for example, one-third of a tenth of the value, then one-third of a tenth of the diyah is obligated; if one-fourth of a tenth, then one-fourth of a tenth. Al-Karkhi said: one looks at the extent of this wound in relation to the mudihah, so that proportion of half a tenth of the diyah is obligated, because what is not specified by text is referred to what is specified. In al-Muhit: the soundest view is to see how much this wound is in relation to the least wound that has a fixed arsh; if it is half of that wound or one-third, then half or one-third of the arsh of that wound is obligated; if one-quarter, then one-quarter. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:443]
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage covers the six minor shijaj (harisah, dami'ah, damiyah, badi'ah, mutlahimah, simhaq), each defined linguistically with reference to the degree of tissue damage. Unlike the major wounds, these have no fixed diyat amounts in the primary sources; therefore, the law requires just compensation (hukumat 'adl) assessed by a qualified expert. The passage then presents the differing Hanafi opinions on how to compute hukumat 'adl. Al-Tahawi's method: compare the depreciation in value of a slave due to the wound, then apply that fraction to the diyah. Al-Karkhi's method: compare the wound's severity to the mudihah, which has a known arsh (half a tenth), and assign a proportional amount. The soundest view according to al-Muhit: compare to the smallest wound that has a fixed arsh (i.e., mudihah) and assign proportionally. This reflects the Hanafi preference for qiyas when textual specification is absent. The passage highlights the ijtihad within the school regarding the calculation, but the underlying principle is unanimous: compensation must be given, and it is based on expert assessment.

Key Terms
حكومة عدل (hukumat 'adl): Compensation assessed by a fair expert, not fixed by text. Methods vary within the Hanafi school.
السمحاق (al-simhaq): The thin membrane (periosteum) between the flesh and skull; a wound reaching it is called simhaq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By comparing the wound's size to the mudihah
B) By assessing the depreciation in a slave's value
C) By comparing to the least wound that has a fixed arsh
D) By a fixed percentage of the diyah

Key Takeaway
For any of the six minor shijaj, the obligation of just compensation (hukumat 'adl) is wajib, and the soundest method calculates it proportionally to the mudihah's fixed arsh.

Qisas for Wounds Below Mudihah: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
States the rule on qisas for shijaj and discusses the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for wounds below mudihah, identify the two narrations from Abu Hanifa, and explain why the Zahir al-Riwayah position is the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Qisas applies to wounds below mudihah because equality can be ensured by probing the depth.

This passage addresses a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the application of qisas for intentional head wounds that do not expose the bone (i.e., below mudihah). While a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad reports that Abu Hanifa held qisas does not apply, the authoritative Zahir al-Riwayah, recorded by Muhammad in al-Asl, states that it does. The reasoning hinges on the possibility of exact retaliation using a probe to measure the wound depth, ensuring equality without risk of bone fracture or death.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for shijaj
Identify the principle: qisas is applicable to mudihah (wound exposing bone) but what about other head wounds? The passage first states a negative rule from one narration.
The initial statement 'there is no qisas in other than mudihah' comes from al-Hasan's narration.
2
Identify the two narrations from Abu Hanifa
Note that there are two conflicting reports: one from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (Nawadir) saying no qisas for below mudihah, and the Zahir al-Riwayah (from Muhammad in al-Asl) saying yes, qisas applies.
The Zahir al-Riwayah is considered more authoritative and is the relied-upon position.
3
Analyse the reasoning of the weaker narration
Al-Hasan's report argues that equality cannot be ensured because wounds below mudihah have no distinct boundary for the knife to follow.
This reasoning would bar qisas for all six minor head wounds (badi'ah, harisah, etc.).
4
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal via tahqiq al-manat
The stronger view holds that equality can be achieved by using a probe (mihbar) to measure the depth, then cutting to that exact depth. Since there is no bone fracture or risk of death, exact retaliation is possible.
This is an example of qiyas with 'imkan al-musawah' as the effective cause.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the weaker narration, the victim would only be entitled to diyah (blood money); under the mufta bihi, qisas is an option for the victim or the state.
The mufta bihi position is the sounder one (al-asahh) and is the operative ruling in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا قصاص في غير الموضحة لأنه لا يمكن اعتبار المساواة فيه؛ لأن ما دون الموضحة ليس له حد ينتهي إليه السكين، وما فوقها كسر العظم، ولا قصاص فيه وهذا رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة رحلته، وفي ظاهر الرواية يجب القصاص فيما دون الموضحة، ذكره محمد له في "الأصل" وهو الأصح؛ لأنه يمكن اعتبار المساواة فيه؛ إذ ليس فيه كسر العظم، ولا خوف التلف فيسبر غورها بمسبار، ثم يتخذ حديدة بقدر ذلك، فيقطع بها مقدار ما قطع، فيتحقق استيفاء
And there is no qisas in other than the mudihah, because equality cannot be ensured therein: since what is below the mudihah has no limit to which the knife can reach, and what is above it involves bone fracture, and there is no qisas in that. This is the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa. In the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), qisas is obligatory for wounds below the mudihah. Muhammad mentioned it in al-Asl, and it is the sounder view, because equality can be ensured therein, since there is no bone fracture nor fear of fatality. One probes its depth with a probe, then takes an iron instrument of that measure, and cuts with it the amount that was cut, thus exacting retribution.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage addresses the application of qisas (retributive justice) for intentional shijaj. The general principle stated is that qisas does not apply to any shajjah other than the mudihah. The commentator explains the reasoning: for wounds less severe than mudihah (i.e., the six minor wounds), there is no precise limit to which the retaliatory cut can be confined, making exact equality impossible. For wounds more severe (hashimah and above), they involve bone fracture, and qisas is not applicable because of the risk of death and difficulty in exact equivalence. However, the passage then reveals an intra-Hanafi dispute. The narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (a Nawadir transmission) reports Abu Hanifa's view that qisas is not applicable to wounds below mudihah. But in Zahir al-Riwayah (the apparent and more authoritative transmission), as recorded by Muhammad in al-Asl, Abu Hanifa's view is that qisas IS applicable for wounds below mudihah, and this is considered the sounder (asahh) position because equality can be achieved by using a probe to measure the depth and then cutting to that exact depth, since there is no bone fracture or risk of death. This demonstrates the Hanafi school's precision in criminal law and the hierarchy of transmissions.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (al-zahir al-riwaayah): The apparent narration from Abu Hanifa through the six books of Muhammad, considered the most authoritative in the Hanafi school.
القصاص (al-qisas): Retributive justice: exacting a similar wound on the perpetrator as was inflicted on the victim.
مسبار (mihbar): A probe used to measure the depth of a wound, ensuring accurate retaliation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because equality cannot be ensured
B) Yes, because equality can be ensured by using a probe
C) Only if the victim demands it
D) No, because it is below the threshold for qisas

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi holds that qisas is wajib for intentional head wounds below mudihah because exact equality can be achieved through probing.

Ruling on the Penetrating Wound (al-Ja'ifah al-Nafidhah)
Ruling on deep wound reaching body cavity with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyah amount for a penetrating ja'ifah and explain the ruling using both the narrated precedent and the rational reasoning.

Core Idea
A penetrating deep wound is treated as two separate wounds, each incurring one-third diyah, totaling two-thirds.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from the chapter of diyat (blood money). The matn states that if a deep wound (ja'ifah) penetrates through the body, the diyah is two-thirds of the full amount. The commentary explains the basis for this ruling: a narrated precedent from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq and a clear rational analogy. Understanding this ruling will help you appreciate how the Hanafi school combines textual proof with logical reasoning to derive legal amounts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the matn: 'If the ja'ifah penetrates, its diyah is two-thirds.' Identify the act (the penetrating wound) and the ruling (obligation of two-thirds diyah).
The ruling is specific to a wound that goes through to the other side.
2
Examine the narrated proof
The commentary cites an athar from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq who judged a similar case with two-thirds diyah. Consider why this precedent is valid in Hanafi fiqh (sunnah of a Rightly-Guided Caliph).
This is a dalil naqli (transmitted evidence) with zanni thubut and dalala.
3
Understand the rational reasoning
The commentator also provides a qiyas: a penetrating wound becomes two separate ja'ifahs (entry and exit). Since each ja'ifah incurs one-third diyah, the total is two-thirds. This strengthens the ruling.
The qiyas is based on the illa of multiple wounds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن نفذت الجائفة فثلثاها التي تقطع الجلد فإن نفذت الجائفة إلخ: فالواجب ثلثا الدية؛ لما روي عن أبي بكر الصديق الله أنه حكم في جائفة نفذت إلى الجانب الآخر بثلثي الدية؛ ولأنها إذا نفذت صارت جائفتين، فيجب في كل واحدة منهما الثلث. (عيني)
If the ja'ifah (deep wound) penetrates, then its [diyah is] two-thirds. (Commentary:) It that cuts the skin – “If the ja'ifah penetrates, etc.”: so the obligatory [diyah] is two-thirds of the full diyah, because of what is narrated from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him) that he judged in the case of a ja'ifah that penetrated to the other side with two-thirds of the diyah, and because when it penetrates it becomes two ja'ifahs, so one-third is obligatory in each of them. (al-'Ayni)
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the penetrating deep wound (al-ja'ifah al-nafidhah). The matn (base text) states: 'If the ja'ifah penetrates, its [diyah] is two-thirds.' The commentary explains that the obligatory amount is two-thirds of the full diyah (blood money). This is based on a narrated precedent (athar) from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him), who judged accordingly in a case where the wound went through to the other side. Additionally, the commentator provides a rational justification: the penetrating wound essentially becomes two separate ja'ifahs (one entering, one exiting), each carrying a one-third diyah obligation, totalling two-thirds. This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school, following the precedent of the Rightly-Guided Caliph.

Key Terms
الجائفة (al-ja'ifah): A wound that reaches the body cavity (jawf), such as the abdomen or chest cavity.
النفذة (al-nafidhah): A penetrating wound that goes through to the other side (e.g., exits from the opposite side).
الدية (al-diyah): Monetary compensation (blood money) paid to the victim or heir for bodily injuries or unlawful killing.
الثلثان (al-thuluthan): Two-thirds of the full diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Six Head Wounds Without Fixed Diyah: Definitions and Ruling
Defining six types of head wounds requiring equitable assessment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define each of the six head wounds (al-harisah, ad-dami'ah, ad-damiyah, al-badi'ah, al-mutalahimah, as-simhaq) and state that they all require hukumat 'adl as compensation.

Core Idea
Six head wounds share one ruling: no fixed compensation, only equitable assessment (hukumat ‘adl).

In this lesson, we examine six specific types of head injuries that fall outside the category of wounds with fixed diyah. The Hanafi jurists have defined each distinct wound by the depth of injury and the nature of bleeding. The common legal consequence is that none of these wounds entitles the victim to a predetermined compensation (arsh) but rather a discretionary amount determined by a qualified expert (hukumat 'adl).

Guided Path
1
Identify the six wounds and their order
List the six Arabic terms from the text: al-harisah, ad-dami'ah, ad-damiyah, al-badi'ah, al-mutalahimah, and as-simhaq. Note that they are respectively the 5th through 10th head wounds in the full classification of eleven.
The first four wounds (mudihah, etc.) have fixed arsh; these six do not.
2
Define each wound by its physical characteristics
For each wound, identify the key feature: e.g., al-harisah scratches the skin without blood; ad-dami'ah shows blood but doesn't flow; ad-damiyah causes flow; al-badi'ah cuts the skin; al-mutalahimah penetrates the flesh; as-simhaq reaches the membrane over the skull.
The definitions rely on observable medical conditions.
3
Distinguish between similar wounds
Compare ad-dami'ah (blood appears but does not flow) with ad-damiyah (blood flows). Similarly, distinguish al-badi'ah (cutting skin) from al-mutalahimah (cutting flesh). Understand why precise classification matters for compensation.
The correct definition of ad-damiyah is debated; the text follows al-Marghinani and Abu 'Ubayd.
4
State the common ruling
All six wounds are subject to hukumat 'adl – an equitable assessment by a trustworthy expert. There is no fixed amount (arsh) prescribed in the texts for these wounds.
The text explicitly says: 'حكمها واحد وهو أنه فيها حكومة عدل' (their ruling is one: in them is hukumat 'adl).
5
Explain the rationale for hukumat 'adl
Because no specific scriptural or analogical basis exists for a fixed arsh for these wounds, yet the harm cannot be ignored, the law requires an expert to assess fair compensation based on circumstance.
This contrasts with wounds like mudihah (exposing bone) which have fixed arsh of five camels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الحارصة إلخ هذه ست شجاج حكمها واحد وهو أنه فيها حكومة عدل فقوله: "في الحارصة" خبر مقدم وما بعده عطف عليه وقوله: "حكومة عدل مبتدأ مؤخر والحارصة هي التي تحرص الجلد أي تخدشه، ولا تخرج الدم مأخوذ من حرص القصار الثوب إذا شقه في الدق، وهي الشجة الخامسة. والدامعة بالعين المهملة هي التي تظهر الدم، ولا تسيله كالدمع في العين مأخوذة من الدمع سميت بها؛ لأن الدم يخرج منها بقدر الدمع من المقلة، وقيل: لأن عينيه تدمعان بسبب ألم يحصل له منها، وهي الشجة السادسة، الدامية وهي التي تسيل الدم، وذكر المرغيناني أن الدامية هي التي تدمي من غير أن تسيل منها هو الصحيح، مروي عن أبي عبيد وهي الشجة السابعة والباضعة وهي التي تبضع الجلد أي تقطعه مأخوذة من البضع، وهو الفصاد الشق، ومنه مبضع الشجة الثامنة، والمتلاحمة وهي التي تأخذ في اللحم فتقطعه كله، ثم يتلاحم بعد ذلك أي يلتئم ويتلاصق سميت بذلك تفاؤلا على ما تؤول إليه، وقال الأزهري: الأوجه أن يقال: اللاحمة أي القاطعة اللحم، وهي الشجة التاسعة والسمحاق وهي التي تصل إلى السمحاق: وهي الجلدة الرقيقة التي بين اللحم وعظم الرأس، وهي الشجة العاشرة. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٤٢/٢]
(Commentary:) “And regarding al-harisah, etc.” – these are six head wounds; their ruling is one: that in them [there is] hukumat 'adl. So his saying: “regarding al-harisah” is a delayed subject and what follows is co-ordinated, and his saying: “hukumat 'adl” is a highlighted predicate. Al-harisah is that which scratches the skin, i.e., abrades it, without drawing blood; derived from the beating of the fuller [when] cutting cloth in clearing; it is the fifth head wound. Ad-dami'ah (with the unpointed 'ayn) is that which shows blood but does not cause it to flow, like tears from the eye; derived from tears; named so because blood comes out like the amount of tears from the eyeball; or because the eyes tear due to pain; it is the sixth wound. Ad-damiyah is that which causes blood to flow; al-Marghinani mentioned that the correct [definition] – transmitted from Abu 'Ubayd – is that it is the wound that makes blood flow without streaming; it is the seventh wound. Al-badi'ah is that which cuts the skin, i.e., slits it; derived from bad' which is phlebotomy/ cutting, hence the lancet; it is the eighth wound. Al-mutalahimah is that which penetrates the flesh, cutting it all, then it heals afterwards (tatalaham); named so optimistically for what it will become. Al-Azhari said: the more appropriate [term] is al-lahimah i.e., the flesh-cutting; it is the ninth wound. As-simhaq is that which reaches the simhaq: the thin skin between the flesh and the skull; it is the tenth wound. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 2/442]
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling exposition for six types of head wounds (shijaj) that are not specifically mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah with fixed diyah amounts. The base text (matn) had listed these six: al-harisah, ad-dami'ah, ad-damiyah, al-badi'ah, al-mutalahimah, and as-simhaq. The commentary defines each term sequentially: 1) al-harisah: a scratch that does not draw blood, 2) ad-dami'ah: blood appears but does not flow (like tears), 3) ad-damiyah: blood flows (the correct definition, per al-Marghinani), 4) al-badi'ah: cuts through the skin, 5) al-mutalahimah: penetrates into the flesh (cutting it completely), and 6) as-simhaq: reaches the thin membrane over the skull. Note that these are 'the fifth through tenth' head wounds in the classification (the first four are not mentioned in this passage). The common ruling for all six is that they have no fixed 'arsh (prescribed diyah) but require 'hukumat 'adl' – an equitable assessment by a qualified expert. This is because there is no scriptural text (sam') for a specific amount, nor can they be completely ignored (avoiding ihtimal harm). The commentary also cites the standard Hanafi reference al-Hidayah for the numbering.

Key Terms
الشجة (al-shajjah): A head wound specifically; used as a general term for wounds on the head.
حكومة عدل (hukumat 'adl): An equitable assessment of compensation by a trustworthy expert, when no fixed diyah is prescribed.
السمحاق (al-simhaq): The thin membrane (periosteum) that covers the skull beneath the flesh; also the wound reaching it.
الأرش (al-arsh): Fixed prescribed compensation for specific bodily injuries, a part of diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the common legal ruling for the six head wounds (al-harisah, ad-dami'ah, etc.)?
A) Each has a fixed diyah of one camel
B) Each requires one-third of full diyah
C) They all require hukumat 'adl (equitable assessment)
D) They are not compensable

Key Takeaway
Six head wounds (al-harisah to as-simhaq) all require equitable assessment (hukumat 'adl) because no fixed compensation is prescribed.

Methods of Hukumat 'Adl: Three Hanafi Approaches
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hukumat 'adl methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi methods for assessing hukumat 'adl and apply the preferred method from al-Muhit.

Core Idea
Hukumat 'adl is wajib, but its calculation is a matter of ijtihad among Hanafi jurists.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on how to compute compensation for wounds that lack a fixed arsh. While all Hanafis agree that hukumat 'adl is obligatory, three different methodologies emerged for determining the amount. Understanding this disagreement reveals how Hanafi jurists applied analytical reasoning (ijtihad) in the absence of a textual ruling, and which method became the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal problem
Recognize that the six head wounds (shijaj) described have no fixed arsh from textual evidence, so they require hukumat 'adl.
The passage begins by affirming the obligation, citing early authorities.
2
State the agreed obligation
All Hanafi jurists agree that hukumat 'adl is wajib; the dispute is only about the method of calculation.
The core idea: wajib but method variable.
3
Understand at-Tahawi's method
At-Tahawi uses market valuation: a slave is valued with and without the wound; the fractional decrease in value determines the fraction of the full diyah owed.
Example: if decrease is 1/30 of value, then 1/30 of diyah is due.
4
Understand al-Karkhi's method
Al-Karkhi analogizes the wound to al-mudihah (a wound exposing bone, fixed arsh = 5 camels). The wound's size relative to mudihah gives the same fraction of its arsh.
Based on the principle: 'what has no text is referred back to what has a text'.
5
Understand the preferred view in al-Muhit
Al-Muhit compares the wound to the smallest wound that has a fixed arsh, taking the same proportion of that arsh. This approach is considered more objective and flexible.
This view is declared 'al-asahh' (most correct) and is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي حكومة عدل إلخ: أي في كل واحد من هذه الشجاج الست حكومة عدل؛ لأنه ليس فيها أرش مقدر من جهة السمع، ولا يمكن إهدارها، فيجب فيها حكومة عدل، وهو مأثور عن إبراهيم النخعي وعمر بن عبد العزيز حملا، واختلفوا في تفسيرها، فقال الطحاوي: هي أن يقوم مملوكا بدون هذا الأثر، ثم يقوم وبه هذا الأثر، ثم ينظر إلى تفاوت ما بينهما، فإن كان ثلث عشر القيمة مثلا يجب ثلث عشر الدية، وإن كان ربع عشر القيمة، يجب ربع عشر الدية، وقال الكرخي ينظر مقدار هذه الشجة من الموضحة، فيجب بقدر ذلك من نصف عشر الدية؛ لأن ما لا نص فيه يرد إلى المنصوص عليه، وفي "المحيط": والأصح أنه ينظر كم مقدار هذه الشجة من أقل شجة لها أرش مقدر، فإن كان مقداره مثل نصف شجة لها أرش أو ثلثها، وجب نصف أوثلث أرش تلك الشجة، وإن كان ربعها، فربع. [رمز الحقائق: ٢/ ٤٤٣]
(Commentary:) “And they are hukumat 'adl, etc.”: meaning, in each one of these six head wounds [there is] hukumat 'adl, because there is no fixed arsh from legal evidence, nor can they be disregarded; thus hukumat 'adl is obligatory. This is reported from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and 'Umar ibn 'Abdul 'Aziz (may Allah have mercy on them). They differed in its interpretation. At-Tahawi said: It is that a slave is valued without this effect, then valued with this effect, then the difference between the two is considered; if the difference is, for example, one-third of a tenth of the value, then one-third of a tenth of the diyah is due; if it is one-quarter of a tenth of the value, then one-quarter of a tenth of the diyah is due. Al-Karkhi said: The amount of this wound is compared to al-mudihah [a wound exposing bone – which has a fixed arsh of 5 camels, i.e., half a tenth of diyah]; in proportion to that, from half a tenth of the diyah [is due], because what has no text is referred back to what has a text. In al-Muhit: The most correct [view] is that one looks at the extent of this wound relative to the smallest wound that has a fixed arsh; if its extent is half of a wound that has an arsh, or a third, then half or a third of the arsh of that wound is due; if it is a quarter, then a quarter. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 2/443]
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the methodology of calculating hukumat 'adl for the six wounds without fixed arsh. It opens by reaffirming that these wounds require hukumat 'adl, citing the authorities of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz. The dispute arises regarding the exact method of assessment. Three positions are presented:

1. Imām al-Ṭaḥāwī's view: Assess the wound by computing the depreciation in market value of a slave with and without the wound. The fractional decrease in value determines the fraction of the full diyah owed. For example, if the decrease is 1/30 of the value, then 1/30 of the diyah is due.

2. Imām al-Karakhī's view: Analogize the wound to the mudihah (which exposes bone and has a fixed arsh of 5 camels, i.e., half a tenth of diyah). The wound is measured proportionately: if it is half the size of a mudihah, half of its arsh (i.e., 2.5 camels) is due; if a third, then a third, etc. This is based on the usul principle: 'what has no text is referred back to what has a text' (al-ard ila al-mansus 'alayh).

3. The preferred view in al-Muhit (and thus the mufta bihi): Compare the wound to the smallest wound that has a fixed arsh (i.e., the mudihah or others like it). Determine the proportion of this wound's size to that of the fixed-arsh wound. Then apply the same fraction to the arsh of that benchmark wound. For example, if the wound is half the size of a mudihah, then half of its arsh (2.5 camels) is due; if a third, then a third; if a quarter, then a quarter. This method is considered more objective and avoids the difficulty of market valuation or the assumption that all wounds must map to the mudihah.

The text thus presents the Hanafi school's reliance on reasoned assessment (ijtihad) in the absence of a nasṣ, with the ultimate authority leaning toward the method in al-Muhit.

Key Terms
الموضحة (al-mudihah): A head wound that exposes the skull bone; its fixed arsh is 5 camels (half a tenth of the full diyah).
نصف عشر الدية (nifs 'ushr al-diyah): Half of one-tenth of the full diyah, i.e., 5 camels (since full diyah = 100 camels).
تفسير حكومة العدل (tafsir hukumat al-'adl): The methodological interpretation of how to assess the equitable compensation for wounds without fixed arsh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in al-Muhit, how is hukumat 'adl calculated for a wound without fixed arsh?
A) By comparing the wound to the mudihah only
B) By estimating the market depreciation of a slave
C) By comparing the wound to the smallest wound that has a fixed arsh and taking a proportional arsh
D) By doubling the arsh of a similar wound

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that hukumat 'adl is wajib, and the preferred method of calculation is proportional comparison to the smallest wound with a fixed arsh.

Qisas for Wounds Below the Mudihah: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qisas for sub-mudihah wounds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi positions on qisas for non-mudihah head wounds and identify which position is mufta bihi and why.

Core Idea
Qisas for non-bone head wounds is wajib in the authoritative Hanafi view; a probe ensures equality.

The base text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that there is no qisas for wounds other than al-mudihah (the one exposing bone). However, the commentary reveals an important intra-Hanafi dispute. One transmission (from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad) denies qisas altogether, but the authoritative transmission (zahir al-riwayah) affirms it. How can equality be ensured when the wound has no natural boundary? The solution is the misbar, a probe that measures depth. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and shows why the majority view prevails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
The matn says 'no qisas in other than al-mudihah.' The commentary explains two views on wounds below the bone (non-mudihah).
The dispute is not about mudihah itself—that clearly has no qisas—but about shallower head wounds.
2
State the minority view and its basis
Narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad: No qisas because equality (musawah) cannot be ensured—the wound has no fixed depth limit, risking excessive harm.
This is a nawadir (rare) transmission, not from the core six books.
3
State the authoritative view and its proof
Zahir al-riwayah (Imam Muhammad in al-Asl): Qisas is wajib (obligatory). Equality can be achieved by measuring depth with a probe (misbar), then cutting with an iron tool of the same length. No bone breakage, no fatal risk.
The commentary declares this view 'al-asahh' (most correct).
4
Explain the practical difference
Under the mufta bihi view, the victim can demand qisas; under the minority view, only diyah (blood money) is available.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (fruit of the dispute).
5
Note the tarjih
The later Hanafi authorities (like the commentator here) adopt the zahir al-riwayah as the operative position, rejecting the nawadir transmission.
No need to mention other schools; this is an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير الموضحة لأنه لا يمكن اعتبار المساواة فيه؛ لأن ما دون الموضحة ليس له حد ينتهي إليه السكين، وما فوقها كسر العظم، ولا قصاص فيه وهذا رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة رحلته، وفي ظاهر الرواية يجب القصاص فيما دون الموضحة، ذكره محمد له في "الأصل" وهو الأصح؛ لأنه يمكن اعتبار المساواة فيه؛ إذ ليس فيه كسر العظم، ولا خوف التلف فيسبر غورها بمسبار، ثم يتخذ حديدة بقدر ذلك، فيقطع بها مقدار ما قطع، فيتحقق استيفاء
(Commentary on the matn: “And no qisas in other than al-mudihah.”) “Other than al-mudihah” because [in a wound below it] equality cannot be considered, because a wound below al-mudihah has no limit for the knife to reach, whereas above it [i.e., al-mudihah itself] is breaking of the bone, and there is no qisas in it. This is a narration from al-Hasan [ibn Ziyad] from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). However, in the manifest narration (zahir al-riwayah), qisas is obligatory for wounds below al-mudihah; Muhammad [al-Shaybani] mentioned it from him in al-Asl, and it is the most correct (asahh), because equality can be considered in it, since there is no breaking of bone and no fear of fatality; so a [probe] is inserted to measure its depth, then an iron [tool] is made to that measurement, and the wound is cut to the amount that was cut, thus achieving [exact] retaliation.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the possibility of qisas (retaliation) for head wounds that are shallower than al-mudihah (i.e., wounds that do not expose bone). The base text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq had stated: 'ولا قصاص في غير الموضحة' (no qisas in other than al-mudihah). The commentary clarifies the different transmissions:

1. Narration of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (nawadir): No qisas is allowed for wounds below the mudihah, because equality (musawah) cannot be ensured — the wound may have no clear boundary, and one risks causing excessive harm or death.

2. Zahir al-Riwayah (the authoritative transmission from the six core books): Qisas is obligatory (yajib) for wounds below the mudihah. This is the position recorded by Imam Muhammad in al-Asl, and it is declared 'asahh' (most correct) in the commentary. The reasoning is that equality is possible: one can measure the depth of the wound with a probe (misbar), fashion a tool of exactly that length, and then cut precisely the same amount of flesh without breaking bone or causing fatal injury. Thus, retaliation is feasible and therefore applied.

The commentary thus presents the standard Hanafi position (as per zahir al-riwayah) as the relied-upon view (mufta bihi), while acknowledging the minority narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (which is not the operative position for fatwa). This demonstrates the relative weight of transmissions within the school.

Key Terms
الموضحة (al-mudihah): A head wound that exposes the skull bone; it has a fixed arsh and qisas is not applicable for it (due to bone involvement).
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The manifest / authoritative transmission within the Hanafi school, as per the six books of Imam Muhammad.
رواية الحسن (riwayat al-Hasan): A narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (a Hanafi authority) that carries less weight than the zahir al-riwayah.
المساواة (al-musawah): Equality in retaliation, i.e., the ability to inflict an identical wound without causing additional harm.
مسبار (misbar): A probe used to measure the depth of a wound to ensure precise retaliation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, is qisas applicable for a head wound below the mudihah?
A) No, because equality cannot be ensured
B) Yes, but only if the victim agrees
C) Yes, because equality can be achieved with a probe
D) No, because such wounds have no fixed arsh

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on qisas for non-mudihah wounds is resolved by adopting the authoritative view that permits exact retaliation using a measurement tool, ensuring justice while minimizing risk.

Rulings on Special Cases: Extra Finger, Infant's Eye, and Qisas in Mudihah
Rulings on diyah/qisas for unusual organs and intentional mudiḥah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for diyah/qisas in cases of extra fingers, child's eye, and intentional mudihah, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference on palm+fingers compensation.

Core Idea
Extra organs and child's eyes demand full compensation if sound; mudihah carries qisas because equality is possible.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses special cases where the standard diyah rules are extended or clarified. You will learn how the Hanafi school treats extra fingers and teeth, the eye of a child, and the intentional head wound known as mudihah. The text also presents an important intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions regarding compensation when the palm is cut along with its fingers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the special cases and their default ruling
List the four special organs mentioned: extra finger, extra tooth, child's eye, child's penis, and tongue. Note that for each, qisas applies if the injury is intentional and the organ is sound. For extra parts, soundness is presumed unless known otherwise.
The phrase 'if its soundness is not known' means we assume full function unless defect is proven.
2
Understand the condition for extra parts
Extra finger and extra tooth are treated like normal ones if they are healthy. If soundness is unknown, it is presumed. If defective, only hukumat (arbitrated compensation) is due.
This applies to both the extra finger (ibn za'idah) and extra tooth (sinn za'idah).
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on palm+fingers
When the palm containing one or two fingers is cut, Abu Hanifa holds that only the diyah for the finger(s) is due (one-tenth each), and nothing for the palm. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say one must consider the arsh of the palm and the fingers, then take the greater amount.
The commentary favors Abu Hanifa's view, citing that fingers are the real origin of benefit and the palm is a follower.
4
Comprehend the proof for qisas in mudihah
The matn states qisas applies in mudihah if intentional. The commentary provides two proofs: a hadith that the Prophet adjudicated qisas in mudihah, and rational reasoning because the knife stops at the bone, making equal retaliation possible.
Mudihah is a head wound that exposes the bone.
5
Recognize why child's eye, penis, and tongue are specified
These organs are singled out because for other parts (hand, foot, nose) the child is treated like an adult in terms of compensation. For the eye, penis, and tongue, the same qisas/diyah rules apply as for an adult when the child is the victim. However, qisas is not enforced against a child perpetrator.
The specification emphasizes that these organs are not subject to any special reduction due to minority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الإصبع الزائدة وعين الصبي وذكره ولسانه إن لم يعلم صحته وكذا السن الزائدة القصاص بذلك، وفي الموضحة القصاص إن كانت عمدا؛ وفي أصابع اليد إلخ:... عشرها: أي عشر الدية في الأصبع. ولا شيء إلخ : أي إذا كان في الكف إصبع أو إصبعان، فقطعهما يجب عشر الدية في الإصبع الواحدة، وخمسها في إصبعين، ولا يجب في الكف شيء، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا ينظر إلى أرش الكف، وإلى أرش ما فيها من الأصابع، فيجب أكثرها، ويدخل القليل في الكثير؛ لأن الجمع بين الأرشين متعذر إجماعا؛ لأن الكل شيء واحد وكذا إهدار أحدهما متعذر أيضا؛ لأن كل واحد منهما أصل من وجه، فرجحنا بالكثرة، ولأبي حنيفة محلله: أن الأصابع أصل حقيقة؛ لأن منفعة اليد وهي البطش والقبض والبسط قائمة بها. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٤٤/٩] وكذا شرعا؛ لأنه أوجب الشرع في إصبع واحدة عشرا من الإبل والكف تبع، فالأصل وإن قل يستتبع الفرع، وإن جل. (عيني) وعين الصبي إلخ: إنما خص عين الصبي وذكره ولسانه؛ لأنه في غيرها من اليد والرجل والأنف كالبالغ، فيجب القود في العمد، والدية في الخطأ. (فتح) صحته أي صحة كل واحد مما ذكر من العين والذكر واللسان. القصاص بذلك، وفي الموضحة القصاص إن كانت عمدا؛ لما روي أنه عليه السلام قضى بالقصاص في الموضحة، ولأن المساواة فيها ممكنة بانتهاء السكين إلى العظم فيتحقق استيفاء القصاص تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٤٢/٩]
(Matn: And concerning the extra finger, the eye of a child, his penis, and his tongue – if their soundness is not known – and likewise the extra tooth, qisas [applies] to that. And in al-mudihah, qisas [applies] if it was intentional. Commentary on the matn) ... (preceding commentary on fingers and palm omitted here) ... “Tenth”: i.e., a tenth of the diyah for the finger. “And nothing” etc.: i.e., if there is in the palm one finger or two fingers, and he cuts them, then one-tenth of the diyah is due for the single finger, and one-fifth for two fingers, and nothing is due for the palm. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: one looks at the arsh of the palm and the arsh of the fingers it contains, then the greater [amount] is due, and the lesser is subsumed in the greater, because combining the two arshs is impossible by consensus, since the whole is one thing; and likewise disregarding one of them is impossible because each one is an origin from one perspective, so we give weight to the greater. Abu Hanifa’s evidence is that the fingers are the real origin, because the benefit of the hand like gripping, clutching, and stretching is accomplished by them. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 9/144] And also legally: because the Law imposed for one finger a tenth of camels, and the palm is a follower; the origin, even if small, carries the branch, however large. (al-'Ayni) “And the eye of a child, etc.”: indeed the eye of a child, his penis, and his tongue are singled out because for other [parts] like the hand, foot, and nose, [the ruling] is like that of an adult: qisas is due for intentional [injury] and diyah for unintentional. (Fath) “His soundness” i.e., the soundness of each one mentioned from his eye, penis, and tongue. “Qisas applies to that, and in al-mudihah qisas applies if intentional; because it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) adjudicated qisas in al-mudihah, and because equality is possible in it with the knife reaching the bone, thus retaliation is achieved. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 9/142]
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage covers several special cases:

1. Extra finger (al-ibn al-za'idah) and extra tooth (al-sinn al-za'idah): The ruling appears to be that if they are healthy (not known to be defective), qisas applies if intentionally cut. The condition 'if its soundness is not known' (in lam ya'lam sihhatuhu) means that we assume soundness until proven otherwise. For extra parts, typically the diyah is a tenth (or pro portion) if there is a fixed amount.

2. Eye of a child, his penis, and his tongue: These are singled out because if these organs are injured in a child, the same compensation applies as for an adult – qisas in intentional injury, diyah in unintentional. The commentary explains that for other parts like hand, foot, nose, the child is treated like an adult in the obligation of compensation (but qisas is not applied to a child who is the perpetrator).

3. Cutting the palm when it contains one or two fingers: This is a separate ruling within the same passage. The matn says 'tenth or fifth' for the finger(s), and 'nothing' for the palm, but only when the palm has fingers (i.e., its function is reliant on them). This is Abu Hanifa's view: the fingers are the real origin, and the palm is subordinate. The two companions disagree, holding that one must consider the arsh of both the palm and the fingers and take the greater amount, because both are origin from different angles. The commentary defends Abu Hanifa's view with both rational and legal arguments: the benefit of the hand (gripping) is achieved by the fingers, and legally the Law assigned a fixed arsh to fingers (10 camels each) but did not assign to the palm; thus the palm is a follower (tabi').

4. Qisas in al-mudihah (wound exposing bone): The matn states 'and in al-mudihah, qisas if it was intentional'. The commentary offers two proofs: a hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) adjudicated qisas in mudihah, and rational reasoning: equality can be achieved because the knife stops at the bone, allowing precise retaliation.

Overall, this passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of handling cases where compensation is not fixed by explicit text (extra parts) or where qisas is potentially applicable.

Key Terms
الإصبع الزائدة (al-ibn al-za'idah): An extra finger beyond the normal five; it may have full diyah if healthy, otherwise only hukumat.
السن الزائدة (al-sinn al-za'idah): An extra tooth; similar ruling to extra finger.
عين الصبي ('ayn al-sabiyy): The eye of a child; the same compensation applies as for an adult, i.e., full diyah for loss of sight.
الموضحة (al-mudihah): A head wound that exposes the bone; intentional mudihah carries qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for cutting the palm that contains one finger?
A) Full diyah for the palm plus diyah for the finger
B) Only the diyah of the finger (one-tenth), nothing for the palm
C) Hukumat 'adl for both
D) Only diyah for the palm, the finger is considered a follower

Key Takeaway
Extra limbs and child's organs are compensated like normal ones if sound; intentional mudihah requires qisas; in palm+fingers, only finger diyah is due per the stronger Hanafi view.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Diyat for Fingers, Palm, and Forearm: The Follower Principle
Rulings on diyat for hand injuries with intra-Hanafi disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyat for cutting fingers with or without the palm and forearm, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the forearm is a follower.

Core Idea
The palm and forearm follow the fingers in diyat unless they have independent significance.

This lesson examines Hanafi rulings on diyat for hand injuries. The key principle is that the palm is a follower of the fingers, so no additional compensation is due for the palm itself. However, the forearm generates dispute: is it a follower or independent? This has practical consequences for blood money.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its base ruling
First, identify the injury: cutting all five fingers of one hand. The base ruling is that each finger carries 10 camels, making the total 50 camels, i.e., half the full diya.
Hadith confirms each finger's diya.
2
Apply the follower principle
If the fingers are cut with the palm, no extra diya is added because the palm is considered a follower of the fingers. The same applies if cut with half the forearm: the fingers and palm still incur half the diya, but the forearm requires hukumat adl according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
Abu Hanifa differs on the forearm.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa considers the forearm a follower, so no separate compensation. The two argue the forearm is an independent part and cannot be ignored, thus requiring hukumat adl.
The text attributes this view to the two; some manuscripts vary.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of the two: hukumat adl is due for the forearm. This is because the forearm has its own function and is not merely a follower.
Later authorities like al-Marghinani endorse this.

(عيني وغيره) عشرها: أي عشر الدية في الأصبع. ولا شيء إلخ : أي إذا كان في الكف إصبع أو إصبعان، فقطعهما يجب عشر الدية في الإصبع الواحدة، وخمسها في إصبعين، ولا يجب في الكف شيء، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا ينظر إلى أرش الكف، وإلى أرش ما فيها من الأصابع، فيجب أكثرها، ويدخل القليل في الكثير؛ لأن الجمع بين الأرشين متعذر إجماعا؛ لأن الكل شيء واحد وكذا إهدار أحدهما متعذر أيضا؛ لأن كل واحد منهما أصل من وجه، فرجحنا بالكثرة، ولأبي حنيفة محلله: أن الأصابع أصل حقيقة؛ لأن منفعة اليد وهي البطش والقبض والبسط قائمة بها. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٤٤/٩] وكذا شرعا؛ لأنه أوجب الشرع في إصبع واحدة عشرا من الإبل والكف تبع، فالأصل وإن قل يستتبع الفرع، وإن جل. أرش الموضحة [ أي دية الذاهب منها فتجب الدية فقط. (طائي)] إذ بفوات العقل تبطل منفعة . جميع الأعضاء، فصار كما إذا أوضحه فمات وأرش الموضحة يجب بفوات جزء من الشعر حتى لو نبت سقط، والدية وجبت بفوات الشعر، وقد تعلقا أي أرش الموضحة والدية بسبب واحد، فدخل الجزء في الجملة كما إذا قطع إصبع رجل، فشلت يده.
والحاصل أن الجناية متى وقعت على عضو واحد فأتلفت شيئين وأرش أحدهما أكثر ، دخل الأقل فيه، ولا فرق بين العمد والخطأ، وإن وقعت على عضوين لا يدخل، ويجب لكل واحد منهما أرشه عمدا كان أو خطأ عند أبي حنيفة؛ لسقوط القصاص به عنده وعندهما يجب للأول قصاص إن كان عمدا، وأمكن الاستيفاء، وإلا فكما قال أبو حنيفة، وقال زفر لا يدخل أرش الأعضاء بعضه في بعض. Book of Diyat: Rulings on Head Wounds. And in the fingers of the hand is half the diya, and if with the palm, and with half the forearm is half the diya and a judgment of equity. And in cutting the palm while it has one or two fingers, a tenth or a fifth of it, and nothing from the joint, while the case is that in it is a fifth of the diya for two fingers in the palm. And for the extra finger, the eye of a child, his penis, his tongue if its health is not known, and likewise the extra tooth, there is retaliation for that. And in the head wound (mudihah), there is retaliation if it is intentional, because it is narrated that he (the Prophet) judged retaliation for the mudihah, and because equality is possible in it by reaching the knife to the bone, so execution of retaliation is realized. Commentary from Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 'And in the fingers of the hand' etc.: meaning in the fingers of one hand is half the diya, because in each finger is ten camels as we narrated, so in five it is fifty necessarily, which is half. 'And if with the palm': this is connected to what precedes, i.e., in the fingers of the hand half the diya, even if he cuts them with the palm, and the blood money does not increase because of the palm, because the palm is a means for the fingers for the right of grasping, for the strength of grasping is by it, so the palm is a follower of the fingers. It is not said: the mention of the fingers here is redundant, because its ruling was mentioned previously; for we say that this issue is mentioned here not to explain itself originally, so that redundancy be imagined, but rather its mention is as a preface for the issue following it, which is his statement: if he cuts them with the palm, there is also half the diya. So the intended meaning in the explanation here is that cutting the fingers alone and cutting them with the palm are equal in ruling. 'And with half the forearm' etc.: meaning in cutting the fingers with half the forearm, i.e., if the fingers are cut with half the forearm, then for the palm and fingers is half the diya as passed, and for half the forearm is a judgment of equity. This is according to them (the two), and according to Abu Yusuf: what is additional to the fingers from the hand and the foot is a follower up to the shoulder and thigh, so the diya does not increase by it, because what has no fixed blood money is a follower of what has fixed blood money. And according to them (the two): the forearm does not follow the palm and fingers, and it cannot be disregarded, so a judgment of equity is due. 'Its tenth': i.e., a tenth of the diya for the finger. 'And nothing' etc.: i.e., if in the palm there is one or two fingers, cutting them requires a tenth of the diya for one finger, and a fifth for two fingers, and nothing is due for the palm. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And they (the two) said: look to the blood money of the palm and to the blood money of the fingers in it, and the greater is due, and the lesser enters into the greater, because combining the two blood moneys is impossible by consensus, since the whole is one thing, and disregarding one of them is also impossible because each of them is an origin in some respect, so we give preponderance by multiplicity. And for Abu Hanifa his analysis: that the fingers are truly the origin, because the benefit of the hand, which is grasping, holding, and spreading, is established by them. And likewise legally, because the law required for one finger ten camels, and the palm is a follower, so the origin, though small, carries the branch, though large. 'And the eye of the child' etc.: he specified the eye of the child, his penis, and his tongue, because in other than these, like the hand, foot, and nose, he is like an adult, so retaliation is required for intentional acts, and diya for error. 'His health': i.e., the health of each of the mentioned: eye, penis, tongue. [Page 435] Book of Diyat: Rulings on Head Wounds: by sight, movement, and speech, a judgment of equity. He wounded a man and his reason or his head hair was lost: the blood money of the mudihah enters into the diya. If his hearing, sight, or speech were lost: no. And if he wounded him with a mudihah intentionally, in the eye, the penis, the tongue. 'And speech': i.e., in the tongue, if he does not cry out as in al-Khaniyya, so full diya is due for the child if he cries out; if he does not cry out, there is a judgment of equity. And in al-Zaylai is something contrary. The upshot is that crying out is like speech in requiring diya according to what is in al-Khaniyya, and al-Zaylai differentiated that crying is not speech, rather a mere sound, and knowledge of health is by speech. 'Judgment of equity': it is raised as a subject, and its predicate is advanced, which is his statement: 'in the extra finger and what follows it is coordinated', i.e., for each of the four mentioned is a judgment of equity. As for the extra finger, because it is a part of a human, so blood money is due for it as honor, even if there is no benefit or beauty in it, like the extra tooth. And retaliation is not due for it, even if the cutter has an extra finger, because their equality is only known by probability, so when retaliation is impossible due to doubt, its blood money is due, and it has no fixed blood money in the law, so a judgment of equity is due. As for the rest, because the purpose of these organs is their benefits; if the existence of benefit is unknown, full diya is not due due to doubt, so a judgment of equity is due. And according to the three (Imams), full diya is due unless it is known that they are not sound. If the health of these organs is known, their ruling is like that of an adult in error and intentional acts, if established by evidence or by the confession of the perpetrator. If he denies and no evidence is established, the statement is the perpetrator's. Similarly, if he says 'I do not know his health', he is not liable for full blood money except by evidence. And the like of the child's penis is the penis of the eunuch and the impotent in ruling and difference. (Fath, Ayni, Mulla Miskin) The blood money of the mudihah [i.e., the diya of what is lost from it, so only the diya is due. (Tai)] because with the loss of reason, the benefit of all organs is nullified, so it becomes as if he wounded him and he died. And the blood money of the mudihah is due for the loss of part of the hair, so if it grows back, it falls, and the diya is due for the loss of hair. And both are connected, i.e., the blood money of the mudihah and the diya, by one cause, so the part enters into the whole, as if he cut a man's finger and his hand became paralyzed. The summary: whenever the injury occurs on one organ and destroys two things, and the blood money of one is greater, the lesser enters into it, and there is no difference between intentional and error. If it occurs on two organs, it does not enter, and each of them requires its blood money, whether intentional or error according to Abu Hanifa, because retaliation is dropped by it according to him. And according to them (the two), retaliation is due for the first if it was intentional and execution is possible; otherwise, it is as Abu Hanifa said. And Zufar said that the blood money of organs does not enter into each other. (Fath, Ayni) 'No': i.e., the blood money of the mudihah does not enter into the blood money of any of these things according to them (the two). And according to Abu Yusuf, it enters into the diya of hearing and speech, but not into the diya of sight, because it is apparent, so it does not join reason. As for hearing and speech, they are hidden, so they join reason, so the blood money of the mudihah enters into them as it enters into the blood money of reason. And according to them (the two), each of these benefits is an origin by itself, so it does not enter. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/444) And it is said in al-Fath in explanation of their school: because they are like different organs, unlike reason, because its benefit returns to all. And it is narrated that Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) judged for one man four full diyas: he struck his head, and his reason, speech, hearing, and sight were lost.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary covers several rulings on diyat (blood money) for injuries to fingers, palm, forearm, and head wounds (shijaj). The author first states that cutting all five fingers of one hand requires half the full diya (50 camels), based on the hadith that each finger has a diya of 10 camels. If the fingers are cut along with the palm, the same half diya applies because the palm is a follower (tabi') of the fingers; the palm itself does not increase the blood money. Similarly, if cut with half the forearm, the half diya applies to the fingers and palm, but the forearm requires a hukumat adl (judgment of just men) because it is not a follower. There is a dispute: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two), the forearm is not a follower and thus requires separate compensation (hukumat adl). According to Abu Hanifa (as reported), the forearm is a follower and no additional diya is due; however, the text here attributes a different view to Abu Yusuf which may be a copyist error, but the key point is the divergence between the imams on whether parts beyond the fingers are followers. The commentary then addresses the case where the palm has one or two fingers: cutting the whole palm with one finger only incurs the diya of that finger (10 camels), and with two fingers, the diya for two fingers (20 camels). Nothing extra is due for the palm itself. This is Abu Hanifa's view. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say that one must consider both the palm's value and the fingers' value and give the greater. Abu Hanifa argues that the fingers are the true origin (asl) because the hand's benefit (grasping) is in the fingers, and the palm is a follower. Next, the text discusses the extra finger, a child's eye, penis, and tongue when the health of these organs is unknown. For such cases, hukumat adl is due, not full diya. According to the three (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali), full diya is due unless the organ is known to be unsound. The Hanafi position is more cautious, requiring proof of health. For the child's tongue, the text mentions a detail: if the child cries (istihlal), it is like speech and full diya is due; otherwise hukumat adl. Then the passage moves to head wounds (shijaj): if a mudihah (wound exposing bone) causes loss of reason or head hair, the arsh (blood money of the wound) is included in the diya for the loss. But if it causes loss of hearing, sight, or speech, the arsh is not included; each is treated separately. There is a dispute among Hanafi scholars: Abu Yusuf includes the arsh in diya for hearing and speech (by analogy to reason), but not for sight. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say no inclusion at all. Zufar says no inclusion between any organs. The general principle derived is: when one injury on a single organ destroys two things, the lesser blood money enters into the greater; if on two organs, they remain separate. This passage is a rich example of Hanafi legal reasoning, combining textual proofs (hadith on fingers), consensus (ijma on impossibility of combining two blood moneys for one thing), analogy (qiyas), and preference (istihsan).

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money, fixed compensation for life or limb.
أرش (arsh): Fixed blood money for a specific limb or wound.
حكومة عدل (hukumat adl): Compensation estimated by two just men when no fixed arsh exists.
تبع (tabi'): Follower; a part that does not have independent legal significance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position, what compensation is due for cutting the forearm along with the fingers?
A) Half diya (50 camels) only, because the forearm is a follower.
B) Half diya (50 camels) plus hukumat adl for the forearm.
C) Full diya (100 camels) because the whole hand is lost.
D) Only hukumat adl for the forearm, with nothing for the fingers.

Key Takeaway
The follower principle simplifies diyat for hand parts, but disputes arise over whether a part is truly a follower or independent, impacting compensation.
