Constructing Structures in Roads: Public vs Private
Initial ruling and commentary on placing structures in public roads

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between public (nafidh) and private (ghayr nafidh) roads in Hanafi fiqh, state the ruling on constructing structures in each, and identify who has the right to demand removal.

Core Idea
In a public road, construction is permissible unless it causes harm; in a private road, unanimous permission is required.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a common urban issue: what structures may be placed in roads and who can object. The ruling hinges on the distinction between public through roads and private cul-de-sacs. Understanding this distinction is essential for applying fiqh in community planning and dispute resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two road categories
Note the terms 'nafidh' (public through road) and 'ghayr nafidh' (private non-through road). The ruling differs fundamentally between them.
A nafidh road leads to a thoroughfare and is public property; a ghayr nafidh road is a dead end owned collectively by residents.
2
State the ruling for public roads
Constructing items like a kanif, mizab, jurs, or dukkan is permissible as long as it does not cause harm to the public. If it causes harm, it becomes haram based on the hadith 'La darar wa la dirar'.
The default is mubah (permissible), but shifted by harm.
3
Identify who can demand removal
Any person entitled to use the road (Muslim, dhimmi, authorized child/slave) can demand removal of a harmful structure. This is based on shared right in public property.
Uninterdicted minors and authorized slaves are included; interdicted ones are not.
4
Distinguish private road ruling
In a private (ghayr nafidh) road, no structure may be built without the unanimous permission of all residents, because the road is jointly owned.
The text says 'la yatasarraf illa bi-idhnihim' – permission from all is required, whether harm occurs or not.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad and the three (Abu Yusuf, Zufar, Hasan) hold that only those actually harmed can demand removal, while Abu Hanifa says any passerby can. The matn supports Abu Hanifa's view.
This ikhtilaf is within the school, not between schools.

[تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٢٦/٩] أو جرصنا: [هو البرج قيل: محرى ماء يركب في الحائط وهو الممر على العلو، وهو مثل الرف، الخشبة الموضوعة على جدار السطحين؛ ليتمكن من المرور، وقيل: هو الذي يعمل قدام الطاقة لتوضع عليه كيزان ونحوها. رمز الحقائق: ٤٤٨/٢] وقيل: هي فلكل نزعه أي لكل واحد من أهل المرور والخصومة مطالبة بالنقض إذا كان ممن يملك التصرف، ولو بالاذن كالمسلم والبالغ العاقل والصبي والعبد المأذونين وكالذمي؛ لأن لكل منهم المرور بنفسه ودوابه، فتكون له الخصومة بنفسه كما في الملك المشترك، بخلاف العبيد والصبيان المحجور عليهم حيث لا يؤمر بالهدم بمطالبتهم؛ لأن مخاصمة المحجور عليهم لا تعتبر في ماله، هذا إذا بنى لنفسه بغير إذن الإمام؛ وإذا بنى للمسلمين كالمسجد ونحوه، أو بنى بإذن الإمام لا ينقض وعند محمد والثلاثة ليس لكل نزعه إذا لم يضربهم، وقال إسماعيل الصفار: إنما ينقض بخصومته إذا لم يكن له مثل ذلك، فإن كان له مثل ذلك لا يلتفت إلى خصومته. (عيني، فتح، تكملة) وله التصرف [ أي للرجل من أصحاب الأملاك] أي له أن يتصرف بإحداث الجرصن وغيره مما تقدم ذكره في الطريق النافذ إذا لم يضر بالعامة ولم يمنعه أحد، فإذا أضر بالعامة لا يحل؛ لقوله علي: "لا ضرر ولا ضرار في الإسلام" تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٦٣/٩] في النافذ الطريق النافذ بإحداث ما ذكر. وفي غيره إلخ : أي في غير النافذ من الطريق لا يتصرف أحد بإحداث ما ذكر إلا بإذن أهله؛ لأن الطريق التي ليست بنافذة مملوكة لأهلها، فهم فيه شركاء، ولهذا يستحقون بها الشفعة والمتصرف في الملك المشترك من الوجه الذي لم يوضع له لا يملك إلا بإذن الكل أضر بهم أو لم يضر، بخلاف النافذ؛ لأنه ليس لأحد فيه ملك، فيجوز الانتفاع به ما لم يضر بأحد، ولأنه إذا كان حق العامة فيتعذر الوصول إلى إذن الكل، فجعل كل واحد كأنه هو المالك وحده في حق الانتفاع ما لم يضر بأحد، ولا كذلك غير النافذ؛ لأن الوصول إلى إرضائهم ممكن، فيبقى على شركته حقيقة وحكما، وفي "المنتقى": إنما يؤمر برفع هذه الأشياء إذا علم حدوثها، فلو كانت قديمة، فليس لأحد حق الرفع، وإن لم يدر حال هذه الأشياء تجعل قديمة، وهذا هو الأصل. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٦٤/٩] إلا ياذهم: بإذن أهل تلك الطريق.
Book of Blood Money. Chapter on what a man may construct in the road. Whoever puts out into a public road a latrine, or an eaves spout, or a projecting shelf, or a shop, then every person has the right to demolish it, and he has the right to act in a through road except if it causes harm, and in a non-through road he may not act except with their permission. 'Ammah (public) means in a through road absolutely whether it harms them or not, from among those who have the right to litigate, even a dhimmi. The chapter on what a man may construct, etc.: when he finished explaining the rulings of direct killing, he began to explain the rulings of killing by causation, and he gave precedence to the former because it is the origin, being killing without intermediary, and because it occurs more often, so there is greater need to know its rulings. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 9/126] Or a projecting shelf: it is a tower, it is said: a water channel placed in the wall, and it is an elevated passage, like a shelf, a wooden plank placed on the walls of two roofs to enable passage, and it is said: it is what is placed in front of a window for placing water jugs and the like. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/448] And it is said: it is. So every person has the right to demolish it, i.e., every one of the passers-by and litigants has the right to demand demolition if he is someone who has the right to act, even by permission, like a Muslim, an adult sane person, a boy, an authorized slave, and a dhimmi; because each of them can pass with himself and his animals, so he has the right to litigate on his own, as in joint ownership. Unlike slaves and minors under interdiction, for they are not ordered to demolish at their demand, because the litigation of those under interdiction is not considered regarding his wealth. This is if he built for himself without the imam's permission; if he built for the Muslims like a mosque and the like, or built with the imam's permission, it is not demolished. And according to Muhammad and the three, not everyone has the right to demolish if they are not harmed. And Isma'il al-Saffar said: it is only demolished by his litigation if he does not have something similar to it; if he has something similar, his litigation is not heeded. ('Ayni, Fath, Takmila) And he has the right to act — meaning the man, the property owner — i.e., he may act by making the projecting shelf and others mentioned in the through road if he does not harm the public and no one prevents him. If he harms the public, it is not permissible; due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'No harm and no reciprocal harm in Islam.' [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 9/163] In the through road: the through road by making what was mentioned. And in other than it etc.: i.e., in the non-through road, no one may act by making what was mentioned except with the permission of its residents; because the road that is not through is owned by its residents, so they are partners in it, and therefore they are entitled to pre-emption via it, and the one who acts in joint property in a manner not designated for it does not have the right except with the permission of all, whether it harms them or not. Unlike the through road because no one has ownership in it, so it is permissible to benefit from it as long as it does not harm anyone. And because since it is a right of the public, obtaining permission from all is impossible, so each one is considered as if he is the sole owner regarding benefit as long as it does not harm anyone. This is not so for the non-through road because it is possible to satisfy them, so it remains in its shared nature in reality and by ruling. And in al-Muntaqa: one is only ordered to remove these things if their recency is known; if they are old, then no one has the right to remove them, and if the state of these things is not known, they are considered old. This is the principle. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 9/164] Except with their permission: with the permission of the residents of that road.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage presents the foundational ruling on constructing structures in roads within the Hanafi school. The ruling distinguishes between two types of roads: nafidh (public through roads) and ghayr nafidh (private non-through roads). For public roads, the owner may place structures such as a latrine, eaves spout, projecting shelf, or shop, provided it does not cause harm to the public. If it causes harm, it becomes impermissible based on the hadith 'La darar wa la dirar' (no harm and no reciprocal harm). Any person who uses the road has the right to demand the removal of a harmful structure. This includes Muslims, adults, sane persons, children, authorized slaves, and even dhimmis, because they all have the right to pass. However, minors and slaves under interdiction cannot litigate in such matters. If the structure was built for public benefit (like a mosque) or with the imam's permission, it cannot be removed. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Imam Muhammad and the three (probably Abu Yusuf, Zufar, and Hasan ibn Ziyad), not everyone has the right to demand removal unless they are actually harmed. Abu Hanifa holds that any passerby can demand removal. For non-through (private) roads, no structure may be built without the unanimous permission of all residents because the road is jointly owned. This is stricter than public roads. Additionally, the commentary notes from al-Muntaqa that old structures (whose recency is unknown) cannot be removed; this is a key practical point.

Key Terms
كنيف (kanif): A latrine or toilet structure placed in the road.
ميزاب (mizab): An eaves spout or gutter projecting from a building into the road.
جرص (jurs): A projecting shelf or balcony; also interpreted as a water channel or tower.
دكان (dukkan): A shop or bench built in the road.
نافذ (nafidh): A public road that leads to a thoroughfare; not privately owned.
غير نافذ (ghayr nafidh): A private road or cul-de-sac owned collectively by its residents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Permission from the imam
B) Permission from all residents of that road
C) Permission from any passer-by
D) No permission is needed as long as it does not cause harm

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of constructing structures in roads depends on whether the road is public (nafidh) or private (ghayr nafidh); in public roads, harm is the key condition, while in private roads, unanimous consent is required.

Liability for Road Obstructions: Indirect Causation and the 'Aqila
Ruling with proof via qiyas on indirect causation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for death or injury caused by road obstructions, identify the conditions for the 'aqila's responsibility, and apply the ruling to various scenarios of indirect causation.

Core Idea
Indirect causation in a public road transfers blood money to the 'aqila, because liability is based on transgression, not direct killing.

This passage from the Book of Blood Money addresses a common urban scenario: someone places a structure in a public road—a latrine, eaves spout, or projecting shelf—and it falls, killing or injuring a passerby. The Hanafi ruling hinges on the distinction between direct and indirect causation. Here, the act is indirect (tasbib), so the blood money falls upon the 'aqila (paternal relatives) of the one who placed it, not on his own wealth. The proof is analogy to digging a well or placing a stone in the road. We will unpack the reasoning, the conditions for liability, and the exceptions when the victim's own deliberate act severs the causal link.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is placing an obstruction or structure in a public road (e.g., a latrine, eaves spout, projecting shelf). The ruling: if a person dies from its fall, blood money (diyah) is wajib upon the 'aqila of the placer. If an animal perishes, the compensation is from the placer's personal wealth.
The ruling is consistent with the original case of digging a well or placing a stone.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author supports the ruling by qiyas (analogy) to the well-known case: 'as if he dug a well in the road, or placed a stone, and a person perishes from it.' That case is established by practice and consensus (ijma). The parallel case (far') includes any structure that falls and kills.
The authority is Zahir al-Riwayah; the ruling is standard in the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the 'illa (effective cause)
The common effective cause is that the act is a transgressive (ta'addi) cause of death in a public road. The placer set in motion a chain of events leading to death without directly killing. This indirect causation (tasbib) triggers liability for diyah but not expiation or inheritance deprivation, because those depend on actual killing.
The distinction between direct and indirect causation is crucial: only direct killing requires kaffarah and prevents inheritance.
4
Identify the exceptions and shurut
Liability only attaches if: (1) the obstruction is placed in a public road (not a private non-through alley), (2) the placer acted without proper authorization (if placed with sultan's permission or on own property, no liability), (3) the victim did not deliberately use the obstruction. If the passerby deliberately passed (e.g., sighted and could avoid), the passerby is the direct cause, and the placer is not liable.
Also, in a desert road, no liability because the victim can avoid it, but in a city, the road is unavoidable.
5
Apply to subsidiary cases
The passage extends the ruling to other acts: throwing dirt, making clay, pouring water, etc. If transgressive, liability arises. Sweeping the road without harmful intent does not incur liability. If one collects sweepings in the road and someone is injured, liability exists because of transgression.
The key principle: liability follows from transgressive causation (ta'addi) in public roads.

كتاب الديات ٤٤٢ ما يحدث الرجل في الطريق فإن مات أحد بسقوطها فديته على عاقلته كما لو حفر بئرا في طريق، أو وضع يجب الدية على العاقلة حجرا فتلف به إنسان، ولو بهيمة فضمانها في ماله، ومن جعل بالوعة في طريق بأمر أي بالبئر أو الحجر المخرج مجمع ماء المطر عام سلطان، أو في ملكه أو وضع خشبة فيها، أو قنطرة بلا إذن الإمام فتعمد رجل جعلها أي في الطريق المرور عليها لم يضمن ومن حمل شيئا في الطريق، حجرا في موضعين بسقوطها: أي بسقوط الميزاب والكنيف والجرصن ونحوها (عيني) فديته إلخ [ أي دية المقتول على عاقلة من أخرج ذلك] أي دية المقتول على عاقلة من أخرج ذلك إلى الطريق؛ لأنه سبب لهلاكه كما يجب الدية على العاقلة لو حفر بئرا في الطريق، أو وضع . را، فتلف بالبئر أو الحجر إنسان؛ لأنه سبب حتى لا تجب الكفارة، ولا يحرم من الميراث، وإنما لم تجب الكفارة، ولم يحرم من الميراث؛ لأن الكفارة والحرمان يعتمدان القتل حقيقة، ولم يوجد، بخلاف الضمان؛ فإنه يعتمد الإتلاف تعديا، وقد وجد. (عيني، فتح) كما لو حفر إلخ اعلم أن المتسبب إنما يضمن في حفر البئر ووضع الحجر إذا لم يتعمد الواقع المرور كذا في "المجتبى"، وفيه حفر في طريق مكة وغيره من الفيافي لم يضمن بخلاف الأمصار قلت: وبهذا عرف أن المراد بالطريق طريق الأمصار دون الفيافي والصحارى؛ لأنه لا يمكنه العدول عنه في الأمصار غالبا. (فتح) ولو بهيمة إلخ : أي لو كان الهالك في البئر أو بسقوط الجرصن بهيمة يكون ضمانها في ماله. [ تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٦٩/٩] لأن العاقلة تتحمل ضمان المال وإلقاء التراب واتخاذ الطين في الطريق بمنزلة إلقاء الحجر والخشبة؛ لأن كل ذلك تسبيب بخلاف ما إذا أكنس الطريق، فعطب موضع كنسه إنسان حيث لم يضمن؛ لأنه غير متعد فيه، ولو جمع الكناسة في الطريق وتلف بها إنسان ضمن؛ لوجود التعدي، وكذا إذا صب الماء في الطريق أو رش أو توضأ، فعطب به إنسان أو حيوان أو مال يضمن؛ لأنه متعد بخلاف ما إذا فعل ذلك في سكة غير نافذة، وهو من أهلها، أو قعد فيه أو وضع خشبة أو متاعا؛ لأن لكل من أهله أن يفعله؛ لأن ذلك من ضرورات السكنى بخلاف الحفر؛ لأنه ليس من الضرورات فيضمن ما عطب .به رمز الحقائق: ٤٤٩/٢] : أو قنطرة: أي وضع قنطرة على نهر رجل المرور بأن كان بصيرا أو يجد موضعا آخر للمرور. لم يضمن أي في الصور كلها أما في البالوعة؛ فلأنه بإذن الإمام أو في ملكه، فليس بمتعد، وأما وضع الخشبة والقنطرة بلا إذن الإمام، وإن كان متعديا فيه، لكن تعمد المار بالمرور عليهما يقطع النسبة إلى الواضع؛ لأن الواضع مسبب، والمار ،مباشر فصار هو صاحب العلة، فلا يعتبر التسبيب معه. رمز الحقائق: ٤٤٩/٢] وأما إذا لم يتعمد المرور بأن كان أعمى، أو مر ليلا، يضمن إذا وضعه بغير إذن الإمام، فأما إذا وضعه بإذن الإمام، فلا يضمن، وكذا إذا حفر في الطريق بغير إذن الإمام فسقط ،ومات يضمن. (عيني، ملا مسكين)
Book of Blood Money. What a man may construct in the road. If someone dies from its fall, then his blood money is on his 'aqila, just as if he dug a well in the road, or placed a stone and a person perishes from it. And if it is an animal, its compensation is in his wealth. Whoever makes a cesspool in the road by order of the sultan, or in his own property, or places a wooden plank in it, or a bridge without the imam's permission, and a person deliberately passes over it, he does not guarantee. And whoever carries something in the road, a stone in two places. By its fall: i.e., the fall of the eaves spout, latrine, projecting shelf, etc. ('Ayni) So his blood money etc.: i.e., the blood money of the killed is on the 'aqila of the one who put it out; because he is a cause of his destruction, just as blood money is obligatory on the 'aqila if he dug a well in the road, or placed a stone, and a person perishes from the well or stone; because he is a cause, so that expiation is not obligatory, nor is he deprived of inheritance. The reason expiation and deprivation are not imposed is that they depend on actual killing, which did not occur. Unlike liability, which depends on harmful destruction, and that occurred. ('Ayni, Fath) As if he dug etc. Know that the indirect cause is only liable in digging a well and placing a stone if the one falling did not intentionally pass. So in al-Mujtaba: digging in the road of Mecca and other deserts does not result in liability, unlike cities. I say: from this it is known that the intended meaning of road is the road of cities, not deserts and open lands, because one cannot avoid it in cities usually. (Fath) And if an animal etc.: i.e., if the perishing animal is in the well or from the fall of the projecting shelf, its compensation is from his wealth. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 9/169] because the 'aqila bears liability for wealth. And throwing dirt and making clay in the road is like throwing a stone or a plank, because all that is indirect causation. Unlike if he sweeps the road, and a person is injured at the place of his sweeping, where he is not liable, because he is not transgressing. And if he collects sweepings in the road and a person is destroyed by it, he is liable because of the existence of transgression. Similarly, if he pours water in the road, or sprinkles it, or performs ablution, and a person, animal, or property is destroyed by it, he is liable because he is transgressive. Unlike if he does that in a non-through alley and he is one of its residents, or sits in it, or places a plank or goods, because each of its residents may do that, since that is from the necessities of dwelling. Unlike digging, because it is not from necessities, so he is liable for what is destroyed by it. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/449) Or a bridge: i.e., he placed a bridge over a river, and the passerby is sighted or finds another place to pass. He is not liable in all these cases. As for the cesspool: because it is with the imam's permission or in his property, so he is not transgressive. As for placing the plank and bridge without the imam's permission, even though he is transgressive in it, but the deliberate passing of the passerby cuts the attribution to the placer, because the placer is an indirect cause, and the passerby is direct, so he becomes the owner of the cause, and indirect causation is not considered with him. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/449) But if he does not deliberately pass, such as being blind or passing at night, then he is liable if he placed it without the imam's permission. If he placed it with the imam's permission, he is not liable. Similarly, if he dug in the road without the imam's permission and someone falls and dies, he is liable. ('Ayni, Mulla Miskin)
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Blood Money deals with liability (daman) when someone dies or is injured due to obstructions or structures placed in public roads. The central ruling is that if a person dies from the fall of a structure like a latrine, eaves spout, or projecting shelf, the blood money (diyah) is upon the 'aqila (the paternal male relatives) of the person who placed it. This is analogized to the case of digging a well or placing a stone in the road, where the 'aqila also pays the diyah. The reasoning is that the act is indirect causation (tasbib), not direct killing. Therefore, no expiation (kaffarah) is required and the killer is not deprived of inheritance, because those consequences only attach to direct killing. However, if the victim is a mere animal, the compensation is from the personal wealth of the causer, not the 'aqila, because the 'aqila only bears liability for human blood money. The passage then details several scenarios: if a cesspool is made with the sultan's permission or on one's own property, there is no transgression and thus no liability. If a wooden plank or bridge is placed without the imam's permission, and a passerby deliberately uses it, the passerby's deliberate act severs the causal link to the original placer, so no liability attaches—the passerby is the direct actor. However, if the passerby did not deliberately use it (e.g., blind or at night), the placer is liable, provided the obstruction was placed without permission. The commentary also distinguishes between cities and open deserts: in a city, one cannot avoid the road, so the causer is liable; in a desert, one can go around, so no liability. Furthermore, the passage discusses other acts like sweeping, throwing dirt, pouring water, etc. Sweeping without harmful intent does not incur liability, but collecting sweepings in the road does, because it is a transgression. The key principle is that liability arises from transgressive causation (ta'addi) in public roads, but in private non-through alleys, residents have more leeway for normal dwelling activities.

Key Terms
عاقلة (aqila): The male paternal relatives of the killer who, in Hanafi law, collectively pay the blood money (diyah) for unintentional killing or indirect causation.
تسبيب (tasbib): Indirect causation, where the actor sets in motion a chain of events leading to death but is not the direct killer.
ضمان (daman): Liability for compensation, typically monetary, for damage or loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi view, why is kaffarah not required when a person dies due to a falling structure in a public road?
A) Because the structure was placed with permission.
B) Because kaffarah is only for intentional killing.
C) Because the death was caused indirectly, not by direct killing.
D) Because the 'aqila pays the diyah, so kaffarah is waived.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, indirect causation of death in a public road places the blood money on the 'aqila of the causer, provided the act was transgressive and the victim did not deliberately expose themselves.
